## Chapter 1 Symmetries and physical fields

I want to start with an introduction to the mathematical language that
will be used to describe physical theories in the chapters 2 , 3 and 4 .
Since the investigation of symmetry structures in these theories has
been my prime motivation to write the thesis, I will devote this chapter
to the action of symmetries on physical fields. I will proceed in four
steps that correspond to the four sections 1.1 - 1.4 of this chapter.

1.  Definition of a physical theory and of a formal symmetry action on
    physical fields. I will show that this naturally leads to a Lie
    structure.

2.  Review of finite dimensional Lie structures, such as Lie groups, Lie
    algebras, their real forms and representations that will be relevant
    for this dissertation.

3.  Discussion of the infinite dimensional Lie algebra of vector fields
    @xmath and its finite dimensional subalgebras that generate @xmath
    by Ogievetsky’s theorem.

4.  An application of this representation theory of real Lie groups
    allows to define an explicit symmetry action on physical fields. I
    close this chapter with the introduction of the vielbein frame.

This thesis will be focussed on the symmetry group of coordinate
transformations @xmath that corresponds to the Lie algebra of vector
fields @xmath . @xmath is closely linked to finite dimensional groups:
In chapter 2 , I will show that gauging the finite dimensional Poincaré
group leads to @xmath -invariant theories. In chapter 3 , I will review
and reinterpret the result of Borisov & Ogievetsky [ 3 ] to construct a
@xmath -invariant theory by requiring invariance under its finite
dimensional affine linear and conformal subgroups alone. And in chapter
4 , I will show that @xmath supergravity seems to be extendable to a
theory in @xmath dimensions, endowed with a particular geometry that
contains a restriction of an infinite dimensional @xmath subgroup to a
finite dimensional one.

Due to this close link between finite dimensional symmetry groups and
infinite dimensional ones, I will discuss all symmetry groups on the
same footing. I will also include a sketch of Cartan’s classification of
finite dimensional, complex, simple Lie algebras, because I want to
highlight the analogy to Ogievetsky’s theorem: in both cases, the
algebras can be descibed by their simpler finite dimensional subalgebras
with relations in between. These are the Serre relations in Cartan’s
case and the vector field representation in Ogievetsky’s. I will provide
further details in section 1.3.4 .

### 1.1 Symmetries in physical theories

#### 1.1.1 Structural remarks

In this dissertation, a physical theory will always denote a collection
of fields @xmath with local dependence on space-time coordinates @xmath
that locally parametrize a real manifold @xmath in @xmath dimensions,
equipped with a non-degenerate metric @xmath of Lorentzian signature.
The explicit dependence of the fields on the coordinates is called the
dynamics of this field theory. It is prescribed by specific partial
differential equations, which are called the equations of motion. Quite
often, these can be derived from an action @xmath by a variational
principle. ¹ ¹ 1 If the theory includes self-dual @xmath -forms, it is
not straightforward to state an action. An example for a case where this
is possible nonetheless is provided in [ 25 ] .

In order to facilitate the analysis, I restrict myself to considering
manifolds @xmath with analytic transition functions [ 62 ] . As
experiments indicate that the concept of spinors is appropriate to
describe physical processes, I further constrain the manifolds @xmath to
have trivial first and second Stiefel–Whitney class [ 41 , 45 ] in order
to be able to define a spin structure. This directly implies
orientability of @xmath .

A theory consists of a collection of fields. In the language of
differential geometry, these are sections of a tensor bundle ² ² 2 This
is not restricted to tensor products of the tangent bundle, the spin
bundle could also be involved. over a manifold @xmath . A simple example
is a scalar field @xmath . This is a mapping from the manifold @xmath to
the field of real numbers

  -- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath      (1.1.1)
  -- -------- -- ---------

As the manifold is analytic, it is natural to require that @xmath
analytically depends on points @xmath . Its precise dependence however,
is only determined by the dynamics, i.e. the equations of motion. More
interesting examples are a vector field @xmath or a tensor field @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
                                   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.1.2a)
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.1.2b)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

To every position @xmath , this vector field @xmath associates a vector
@xmath in the tangent space @xmath and @xmath a tensor @xmath in the
tensor product of two cotangent spaces. Examples for these objects are
the electromagnetic potential @xmath and the metric @xmath , if the
tensor product @xmath is symmetric.

The simplest example of a Lorentzian manifold @xmath , i.e. a manifold
@xmath with a non-degenerate metric @xmath of Lorentzian signature, is
flat Minkowski space

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.1.3)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

In this special case, the manifold is a vector space. This implies that
the linear span of an arbitrary choice of linearly independent basis
vectors @xmath with @xmath generates the manifold

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.1.4)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

Every point on @xmath is hence uniquely determined by the coefficients
@xmath with respect to this global basis:

  -- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath      (1.1.5)
  -- -------- -- ---------

This defines a coordinate system on @xmath , which is globally defined.
For a general manifold @xmath , e.g. the @xmath -sphere @xmath , the
situation is different: there is no distinguished set of coordinate
systems as in the Minkowski case such that the entire manifold @xmath
can be covered by a single coordinate system. ³ ³ 3 There also are
curved manifolds that have this property, e.g. the @xmath sphere @xmath
. These are called parallelizable manifolds that are important for the
teleparallel interpretation of general relativity addressed in section
2.2.5 . However, there is a remnant of this structure: by the very
definition of a differentiable manifold, for any point @xmath , there is
an open neighbourhood @xmath with @xmath which is diffeomorphic to a
vector space. The important insight is that one can still use the
coordinate basis introduced in ( 1.1.4 ), if the global validity is
changed to a local one and if the metric @xmath is replaced by the
Lorentzian metric @xmath ( 1.1.2b ). Technically, this is done by
substituting the open neighbourhood @xmath for @xmath in the definitions
above and using the appropriate form of @xmath in local coordinates.
This procedure will be of prime importance for the discussion of
non-linear realizations in chapter 3 , where I will work with a fixed
coordinate system, i.e. with fixed vectors @xmath ( 1.1.4 ) whose linear
span is diffeomorphic to the open set @xmath .

#### 1.1.2 Symmetries of a physical theory

After these first structural remarks I want to introduce the notion of
symmetry in a physical theory: a symmetry maps one set of physical
fields to another one, with both sets satisfying the equations of
motion. In this dissertation, I want to distinguish two possible ways a
symmetry can act on physical fields.

1.   External symmetries :
    An external symmetry acts on the coordinates and thus induces an
    action by the relevant bundle structure on the physical field. Even
    for a scalar field @xmath ( 1.1.1 ), this action is not trivial.
    This immediately follows from a Taylor expansion about the
    coordinate @xmath . For a general manifold, these transformations
    are diffeomorphisms and illustrate only the arbitrariness of the
    choice which local coordinate system has been fixed. Sometimes, they
    are also referred to as space-time symmetries.

2.   Internal symmetries :
    An internal symmetry only acts on the fibres of the tensor bundle
    and not on the manifold directly. In particular, it does not act on
    the coordinates. A simple example is provided by the system of two
    scalar fields with identical dynamics and the symmetry would be a
    mixing of the two.

For both kinds of symmetries, the following definition applies: if the
same transformation is considered for all points @xmath , the symmetry
is called global . If this is not the case, one speaks of a local or
soft symmetry. The process of making a global symmetry local is known as
gauging this global symmetry. This will be the main topic of chapter 2 .

In chapters 4 and 5 , I will address the question whether an (in this
sense) internal symmetry of supergravity, the gauge symmetry of the
@xmath -form potential, is in fact an external symmetry in an extended
picture with additional coordinates, on which these gauge
transformations act in a non-trivial way. I want to emphasize that this
interpretation is not in conflict with the Coleman-Mandula theorem [ 24
] , because the geometry is enlarged.

Before discussing the symmetry action on physical fields in more detail,
I want to mention a possible source of confusion: the discrimination
between external and internal symmetries is not equivalent to the
distinction between physical symmetries and gauge symmetries in the
setting of general relativity. Gauge symmetries always are external
symmetries, but physical ones can be of external or internal type. ⁴ ⁴ 4
An example for a gauge symmetry would be any isometry of the spacelike
hypersurface @xmath in the de Sitter solution in @xmath . As @xmath does
not have a boundary, the Noether charges, e.g. the ADM-mass for
asymptotically flat manifolds [ 1 ] , are not affected. Hence, the
solution is not changed by the symmetry action. For a spacelike
hypersurface with non-trivial boundary, however, the Noether charges
would be affected, which may lead to a different solution. This is an
example for a physical symmetry. For this thesis however, these notions
will not be important.

#### 1.1.3 Formal symmetry action on a physical field

Let @xmath denote the physical field that solves the equations of
motion. For this thesis, I want to define the action of a symmetry
@xmath on @xmath to depend analytically ⁵ ⁵ 5 More general symmetries,
e.g. discrete symmetries, are interesting in their own right, but I will
not treat them in this thesis. on a (multi)label @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.1.6)
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

In general, a physical theory contains more than one field. For these
cases, @xmath is an abbreviation for the set they create. Following the
definition from section 1.1.2 , the physical field @xmath also solves
the equations of motion. This defines an equivalence relation

  -- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath      (1.1.7)
  -- -------- -- ---------

The action of the same symmetry with a different (multi)label @xmath on
@xmath results in

  -- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath      (1.1.8)
  -- -------- -- ---------

Hence, I can define a third (multi)label @xmath for this symmetry by the
concatenation of the two symmetry transformations

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.1.9)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

Thus, I have defined a product structure on the space of symmetry
transformations. Due to the continuous dependence on the (multi)label
@xmath , there is a unity element @xmath , i.e. a trivial symmetry
transformation. And as the two equivalence relations @xmath and @xmath
should have the same meaning, there should also be an inverse symmetry
transformation. In other words, for every (multi)label @xmath there is
another label @xmath such that @xmath in ( 1.1.9 ). Finally, consider
the chain of symmetry transformations linking the four solutions @xmath

  -- -------- --
     @xmath

Following ( 1.1.9 ), this defines two symmetry transformations @xmath
and @xmath . Hence, there are two possibilities to define a symmetry

Requiring a unique symmetry transformation that links the two solutions
@xmath and @xmath is equivalent to demanding associativity of the
product

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.1.10)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

Therefore, the space of analytic symmetry transformations exactly
reproduces the definition of a Lie group . The set of all physical
fields satisfying the equations of motion, forms an orbit of the Lie
group action. The remaining part of this chapter is devoted to this
topic. The reason for this is that symmetries play a crucial role in
@xmath supergravity, whose structures I will investigate in chapter 4 .
In passing, I introduce the following definitions:

###### Definition 1.1

Let @xmath be objects, on which symmetry transformations can act, such
as solutions of physical theories, their equations of motion or actions.

-   @xmath is called invariant under a symmetry action @xmath if

      -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
         @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.1.11)
      -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

    As an example may serve the Ricci scalar ( 2.2.12 ) that is
    invariant under a general coordinate transformation @xmath .

-   @xmath is called covariant under a symmetry transformation if the
    induced action on @xmath is (multi)linear. An example is the
    transformation of Einstein’s equations of motion under a general
    coordinate transformation @xmath : the induced action is a
    multiplication with the Jacobi matrix of the diffeomorphism @xmath
    of both free indices. ⁶ ⁶ 6 Sometimes, @xmath is called a tensor, if
    it fulfills this property. I do not use this name at this point,
    because it is also used in contradistinction to “spinor” in the
    physics literature, which also is a covariant object. I will come
    back to this subtlety in section 1.2.5 .

-    A physical theory shall be called invariant under a symmetry
    transformation, if its equations of motion are covariant. ⁷ ⁷ 7 If
    an action @xmath exists, this is equivalent to an invariance of
    @xmath modulo a constant rescaling.

-    A covariant derivative or connection @xmath acting on a covariant
    object @xmath is called equivariant under a symmetry transformation,
    if @xmath also is covariant.

The last definition may require some further explanation: I have defined
a physical theory by its equations of motion that are differential
equations of the physical fields. Furthermore, I have already mentioned
in section 1.1.2 that a symmetry transformation can be @xmath
-dependent, in particular if it is gauged. If I want both the physical
fields and the equations of motion to be covariant objects, it is
natural to replace the differential operator @xmath by a connection
@xmath that is equivariant under the symmetry transformation.

### 1.2 Lie theory

#### 1.2.1 Lie groups and Lie algebras

After this physical introduction to the Lie structure, I want to
formalize this to make contact with the mathematical literature [ 62 ,
18 ] .

###### Definition 1.2

A Lie group @xmath is a differentiable manifold which is also endowed
with a group structure such that the map

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

is differentiable.

As the group multiplication always is associative, a short comparison of
this definition with section 1.1.3 indeed shows that the symmetry
structure of a physical theory is provided by a Lie group. The basic
building blocks of finite dimensional Lie groups, i.e. of symmetry
groups with finitely many symmetry generators, can be classified. This
is done by the relation to Lie algebras:

###### Definition 1.3

Let @xmath be a field of characteristic @xmath . A Lie algebra is a
@xmath -vector space @xmath with a bilinear ( 1.2.2a ), antisymmetric (
1.2.2b ) multiplication @xmath satisfying the Jacobi identity ( 1.2.2c
):

  -- -------- -- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath      @xmath      (1.2.1)
  -- -------- -- -------- -- ---------

For all @xmath and for all @xmath , this implies

  -- -------- -- ----------
                 
     @xmath      (1.2.2a)
     @xmath      (1.2.2b)
     @xmath      (1.2.2c)
  -- -------- -- ----------

Lie algebras @xmath are linked to Lie groups @xmath by the exponential
map

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.2.3)
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

The local diffeomorphism @xmath is a homomorphism mapping the additive
structure of the Lie algebra @xmath to the multiplicative structure of
the Lie group @xmath . ⁸ ⁸ 8 The Lie product ( 1.2.1 ) is linked to the
group multiplication ( 1.1.9 ) and the Jacobi identity ( 1.2.2c ) to the
associativity ( 1.1.10 ). Note that @xmath is not a global
diffeomorphism in general. I will comment on this in section 1.2.5 . In
the classical domain of physical theories, the real numbers @xmath are
the relevant choice for the field @xmath . I will start the
classification with the complex numbers @xmath in the next section
before refining to real forms in section 1.2.4 .

#### 1.2.2 The classification of simple, finite dimensional Lie algebras

The main aim of this section is to motivate the way this classification
of complex Lie algebras is proved [ 18 ] . In chapter 4 , I will need
this argumentation to identify the geometric structures that are linked
to @xmath supergravity.

###### Definition 1.4

Let @xmath be a finite dimensional, complex Lie algebra. There is a
natural action of @xmath on itself, defined by

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

This is called the adjoint action of the Lie algebra @xmath on the
vector space @xmath . The Cartan subalgebra @xmath is defined to be the
maximal Abelian diagonizable subalgebra of @xmath . Its dimension is
called the rank of the Lie algebra @xmath .

In order to define the roots of a Lie algebra, I restrict the adjoint
action to the Cartan subalgebra

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

As all elements of the Cartan subalgebra @xmath commute, Schur’s lemma
and the Jacobi identity ( 1.2.2c ) imply that all the maps @xmath
commute. Hence, there is a common eigenvector of @xmath . The
eigenvalues however, do depend on the element @xmath . This mapping is
called the root of a Lie algebra:

###### Definition 1.5

For a given eigenvector @xmath of the adjoint action @xmath , the
eigenvalues are given by the functional

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.2.4)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

For any eigenvector @xmath , the non-zero functionals @xmath are called
roots or equivalently weights of the adjoint representation .

A further necessary definition is the one of a simple Lie algebra:

###### Definition 1.6

A simple Lie algebra @xmath has no non-trivial ideal. ⁹ ⁹ 9 This is
equivalent to stating that a simple @xmath does not have a proper
subspace @xmath with the property @xmath for all @xmath and all @xmath .

With a short calculation using the Jacobi identity ( 1.2.2c ), one can
prove that if @xmath and @xmath are different roots, @xmath also is a
root, if the corresponding common eigenvector @xmath does not vanish.
This is one of the most important insights in Lie theory. Together with
the finite dimensionality, it results in the fact that there must be a
highest root [ 18 ] . Hence there are finitely many roots for all finite
dimensional Lie algebras, which introduces a discrete lattice structure,
the root lattice, for a Lie algebra. This is the starting point of the
classification and the result is the following table:

+-----------------------------------+-----------------------------------+
| @xmath                            | []                                |

+-----------------------------------+-----------------------------------+

+-----------------------------------+-----------------------------------+
| @xmath                            | []                                |

| @xmath                            | []                                |
+-----------------------------------+-----------------------------------+

+-----------------------------------+-----------------------------------+
| @xmath                            | []                                |
+-----------------------------------+-----------------------------------+

Every node in these diagrams is labeled by a root @xmath and corresponds
to an @xmath algebra in the following sense: For the node with label
@xmath with @xmath , one associates three vectors @xmath , a so-called
Chevalley triple forming an @xmath subalgebra of @xmath . Its
commutation relations are

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- --
                                   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- --

The @xmath commuting generators @xmath form the Cartan subalgebra @xmath
of @xmath . The complete Lie algebra @xmath is a subspace of the free
algebra , which is the vector space generated by the @xmath vectors
@xmath and arbitrary commutators thereof. The precise embedding of
@xmath in the free algebra is provided by the relation between the
different @xmath subalgebras, i.e. the commutators of @xmath with @xmath
for @xmath . These are encoded in the Dynkin diagram or equivalently, in
the Cartan matrix @xmath : ¹⁰ ¹⁰ 10 For the cases discussed here, @xmath
is an @xmath matrix with @xmath on the diagonal. If the nodes @xmath and
@xmath are linked with one line, @xmath . If there are two or three

All the other elements are zero. For a generalization, consult [ 38 ] .

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.2.6)
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.2.7)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

To sum up, every simple, finite dimensional, complex Lie algebra @xmath
is the quotient space of the corresponding free algebra modulo the Serre
relations ( 1.2.7 ), modulo the commutation relations @xmath and modulo
( 1.2.5 ). @xmath is hence uniquely characterized by a Dynkin diagram or
equivalently, by its commution relations.

This property is the starting point of Kac-Moody theory [ 36 ] . I have
already mentioned above that the derivation of the discrete structure of
Lie theory heavily relies upon the finite dimensionality of the
algebras. In discussing infinite dimensional Lie algebras, Kac and Moody
realized that postulating the Serre relations ( 1.2.7 ) for an infinite
dimensional Lie algebra also leads to a very interesting mathematical
structure. This can also be encountered in physical theories. An example
is the Geroch group [ 5 , 19 ] , which is a symmetry acting on the space
of solutions of General Relativity with two commuting, independent
Killing vectors. ¹¹ ¹¹ 11 Its corresponding Lie algebra @xmath [ 34 ]
possesses two @xmath subalgebras, whose corresponding real groups are
called Ehlers and Matzner-Misner group respectively. The
Kramer-Neugebauer transformation from General Relativity [ 15 ]
corresponds to the automorphism of the Dynkin diagram of @xmath .

As the Serre relations provide the same information as the Dynkin
diagram, any Kac-Moody algebra is uniquely defined by its Dynkin
diagram. However, note that in contradistinction to the finite
dimensional case, this subset of infinite dimensional Lie algebras does
not contain all physically interesting structures: the Virasoro algebra
of @xmath conformal field theory and the algebra of all vector fields
are two very prominent examples for infinite dimensional Lie algebræ of
non-Kac Moody type, i.e. they do not have an associated Dynkin diagram.

#### 1.2.3 Representation theory

I have defined physical fields to be sections of a tensor bundle over a
@xmath -dimensional manifold. This implies that for every point @xmath ,
the physical field can be written as a tensor product of vector spaces.
Therefore, the induced action of a symmetry on these vector spaces has
to be a linear one. Hence, one is led to the definition of a linear
representation:

###### Definition 1.7

#### 1.2.5 Representations of Lie groups

It follows from the classification of irreducible representations in
section 1.2.3 that every irreducible representation of @xmath is a
subrepresentation of a (multiple) tensor product of the @xmath
-dimensional vector representation @xmath and its dual or contragredient
representation @xmath [ 18 ] . This is the reason why @xmath and @xmath
are referred to as fundamental and antifundamental representations of
@xmath . Since the orthogonal algebra @xmath is a subalgebra of @xmath ,
every representation of @xmath also is a representation of @xmath .
There are however representations of @xmath that are not representations
of @xmath . These are the spin representations that are related to
representations of the Clifford algebra [ 18 , 69 ] , which will be
important for the discussion of fermions in physical theories in
chapters 2 and 4 .

For the representation of Lie groups, recall from section 1.2.1 that the
homomorphism @xmath ( 1.2.3 ) locally maps the Lie algebra @xmath to a
corresponding group @xmath . For the finite dimensional Lie algebras,
@xmath is the standard matrix exponential. Thus, every representation
@xmath of a Lie algebra @xmath induces a representation of a simply
connected Lie group @xmath [ 44 ] . As @xmath merely is a local
diffeomorphism, the Lie groups @xmath and @xmath may have different
topologies. For @xmath , it is standard to denote the simply connected
Lie group @xmath by @xmath . For @xmath , this is not the case: @xmath
is by definition a subgroup of @xmath . This is the reason why @xmath is
not simply connected. Therefore, the spin representations are not
representations of @xmath , but representations of its simply connected
covering group ¹⁶ ¹⁶ 16 The discussion of these discrete subgroups has
often been disregarded in the literature in the past, e.g. in the
context of possible symmetry groups @xmath or @xmath of M-theory. By
referring to these discrete subgroups, these conjectures could be
strongly constrained [ 38 ] .

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

It also is standard to reserve the notion tensor representation for
@xmath representations. @xmath or @xmath representations that are not
tensors in this sense are called spinors . This is the reason why I have
not called @xmath a tensor in the definitions of section 1.1.3 . In
other words, only representations with an even number of spinor indices
are representations of the group @xmath , because an even number of
spinors can always be transformed into tensor representations by the
Fierz identity.

This fact that spinors are not a representation of the Lorentz group
@xmath may have led some authors to the conclusion that spinors must be
described by infinite dimensional spin representations in the context of
non-linear realizations [ 39 ] . Since all physical expectation values
are of even degree in fermions, the Fierz identity argument applies,
however. Hence, it is perfectly consistent to describe physical objects
as @xmath equivalence classes of spinors, on which a Lorentz group
action is well-defined. It is common in the physics literature not to
mention this subtlety explicitly and just to talk about an induced
Lorentz action on spinors instead. I will adopt this convention in the
chapters 2 and 3 .

For the discussion of supergravity in chapter 4 , this distinction will
also be important. Therefore I want to add an easy prescription how to
identify the topology of a subgroup. Let @xmath be the maximal compact
subgroup of a Lie group @xmath . Then all admissible @xmath
representations arise from a decomposition of all admissible @xmath
representations under @xmath . For the case @xmath , the fundamental
representation @xmath decomposes into the @xmath representations @xmath
. This implies that all the admissible representations of @xmath have an
even number of @xmath indices: the discrete group action that multiplies
every index with a minus sign can hence be divided out. In the context
of supergravity in chapter 4 , this implies that @xmath is the
covariance group of the bosons, whereas its simply connected covering
group @xmath is the covariance group of the fermions.

### 1.3 Diffeomorphisms

I have already discussed in the context of Dynkin diagrams in section
1.2.2 that a Lie algebra @xmath can often be described as the free
algebra of @xmath subalgebras modulo some relations. Most prominent
among these are the Serre relations ( 1.2.7 ) for the case of (infinite
dimensional) Kac-Moody algebras. This section will deal with a
non-Kac-Moody algebra corresponding to a subgroup @xmath of the group of
diffeomorphisms on a manifold. This can also be constructed from two
finite dimensional subalgebras by Ogievetsky’s theorem [ 51 ] , which
can also be interpreted as defining relations between the two
subalgebras with the help of their vector field representation @xmath .

I will start with the geometric origin of the symmetry group of
diffeomorphisms in physical theories. Then, I will restrict this group
to the subgroup @xmath that corresponds to the Lie algebra of analytic
vector fields @xmath in section 1.3.2 . After discussing its relevant
subgroups in 1.3.3 , I will prove Ogievetsky’s theorem in 1.3.4 .

#### 1.3.1 Diffeomorphisms in physical theories

I have defined physical fields in section 1.1.1 to be sections of a
tensor bundle over an analytic manifold @xmath . By definition [ 62 ] ,
@xmath is equipped with an atlas of coordinate charts @xmath that

mappings

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.3.1)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

For every open set @xmath , the coordinate charts @xmath provide

that there are many choices for a coordinate chart. The content of a

coordinate chart has been chosen. This is referred to as general

equivalently. In formulæ, for every point @xmath and for two arbirary
charts @xmath and @xmath with @xmath , the diffeomorphism @xmath is a
map from @xmath to @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.3.2)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

Hence, for the discussion of a physical theory with local dependence on
@xmath , it is no restriction to fix one coordinate chart @xmath , if
the equations of motion are covariant under the symmetry action of
diffeomorphisms. ¹⁷ ¹⁷ 17 This covariance follows the definition of
section 1.1.3 . In other words, a solution to the equations of motion
has to be identified with all solutions that are generated by the action
of a diffeomorphism. This implies that I can without loss of generality

#### 1.3.2 The group of diffeomorphisms @xmath

The requirement of locality for a physical theory, which is standard for

     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath

#### 1.3.4 Ogievetsky’s theorem

I want to start this section with the observation that all generators in

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

@xmath and @xmath in their vector field representation @xmath . These

and the conformal ones ( 1.3.22b ), respectively.

In order to show that this closure with respect to the Lie bracket of

analytic functions. This implies that the vector space of analytic
vector fields @xmath is spanned by the closure of the vector fields

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.3.36)
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

The basis vectors @xmath of @xmath have a vector field representation

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (1.3.37)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

as homogeneous monomials of degree @xmath . The sum over @xmath in (
1.3.36 ) is a sum over @xmath , but it also contains a sum over the
partitions of @xmath in @xmath . ²⁴ ²⁴ 24 This is a Hamel basis, not a
Schauder basis, i.e. for every analytic vector field @xmath there are
coefficients @xmath such that ( 1.3.36 ) holds. Given @xmath , the
number of non-vanishing coefficients @xmath does not have to be finite,

###### Theorem 1.10

For @xmath , the vector field representation @xmath of any basis
monomial @xmath ( 1.3.37 ) is a linear combination of the affine linear
generators @xmath ( 1.3.14 ), the conformal ones @xmath ( 1.3.24 ) and
arbitrary commutators thereof in their representation @xmath as vector
fields.

###### Proof.

Following Ogievetsky [ 51 ] , I prove this theorem in detail in the
appendix 6.1.5 . It contains two parts: At first, I show that a general
vector field ( 1.3.37 ) of polynomial degree @xmath is contained in the
closure. Then, I use this to construct vector fields @xmath of arbitrary
degree @xmath by induction.∎

It is interesting to observe that not all conformal vector fields are
necessary for the theorem to hold. This allows to refine the theorem by
the following corollary that I prove in appendix 6.1.6 .

###### Corollary 1.11

For @xmath , fix @xmath arbitrary. Then the vector field representation
@xmath of any basis vector @xmath ( 1.3.36 ) in @xmath dimensions is a
linear combination of (commutators of) the affine linear generators
@xmath ( 1.3.14 ) in @xmath dimensions and the conformal ones @xmath (
1.3.24 ) in @xmath dimensions in their representation @xmath as vector
fields.

Ogievetsky’s theorem 1.10 is the starting point of the procedure of
Borisov & Ogievetsky to generate a gravitational theory from the
simultaneous non-linear realizations of the affine linear and the
conformal symmetry group, which will be the topic of chapter 3 . From
the details of the proofs, I want to highlight the following points:

-   It is interesting to ask if the Lie algebra @xmath is generated by
    the closure of the affine algebra @xmath with an arbitrary vector
    field whose dependence on the coordinates is quadratic or higher.
    This is not true: the vector field @xmath is quadratic in @xmath ,
    but it transforms as a vector representation under @xmath and it
    also is Abelian. To generate @xmath , it is essential to include
    vector fields like @xmath ( 1.3.24 ) in the closure that do not
    transform as a linear representation of @xmath .

-   The affine algebra @xmath does not preserve the structure defined by
    the symmetric form @xmath ( 1.3.19 ), which could be interpreted as
    the reason why the commutator maps outside the conformal algebra
    @xmath .

-   This implies in particular that the signature @xmath of the flat
    metric @xmath had no effect on the proof of Ogievetsky’s theorem.
    There is no connection to the signature of the metric @xmath on the
    Lorentzian manifold @xmath .

-   It is only for the construction of a gravitational theory à la
    Borisov & Ogievetsky that it is necessary to endow the conformal
    vector field ( 1.3.22b ) with the Lorentzian signature @xmath .

To conclude, I want to close the loop of the analogy of Ogievetsky’s
theorem to the classification of simple, complex, finite dimensional Lie
algebras from section 1.2.2 . In both cases, the precise way how the
subalgebras are linked to each other is of crucial importance. As an
example, the reader may consider the two Kac–Moody algebras @xmath and
@xmath : both have two @xmath subalgebras, but @xmath is finite
dimensional and @xmath is not. This is due to the fact that the two
@xmath -subalgebras are linked by different Serre relations ( 1.2.7 ).
In the case of @xmath , the corresponding relation is provided by the
vector field representation @xmath of @xmath ( 1.3.14 ) and @xmath (
1.3.24 ), whereas for the embedding of both algebras in e.g. @xmath ,
the relations would be different.

### 1.4 Application to physical fields

#### 1.4.1 Explicit symmetry action on a physical field

#### 1.4.2 The connection @xmath on fields

#### 1.4.3 The vielbein frame

-   The frame @xmath is not coordinate induced in general. In other

-   The vielbein frame is a special case of a @xmath structure as

In relating physical theories to experiments the flat Minkowski space
@xmath ( 1.1.4 ) plays a distinguished role. For this particular

## Chapter 2 The dynamics and why @xmath supergravity?

In this chapter, I will introduce the dynamics of the theories relevant
for this dissertation. I have closed the previous chapter with the
remark that the flat Minkowski space @xmath ( 1.1.3 ) plays a prominent
role in relating physical theories to experiments. I will start by
providing an example for such a physical theory in the first part: the
gauging of the global, internal @xmath symmetry of a free Dirac field
and its quantization that leads to quantum electrodynamics. The big
advantage of describing an observable force by a gauge theory lies in
the possibility to use the concept of a perturbation expansion in its
domain of validity to relate the theory to experiments. ¹ ¹ 1 The
restricted domain of validity of the perturbation expansion emphasizes
the interpretation of quantum electrodynamics as an effective field
theory: neither for very large energies nor to infinite accuracy, it is
adequate to use the tool of perturbation expansion, which is an
asymptotic series in general [ 70 ] .

This is the reason to devote the second part of this chapter to the
gauging of the global, external Poincaré isometry group of Minkowski
space. This procedure naturally leads to a gravitational theory with
torsion [ 60 ] , a so-called Einstein–Cartan theory such as
supergravity. Thus, it restores the property of the metric being a
dynamical object, which was dropped in establishing the contact to
experimental physics to begin with. Where the role of torsion is
concerned, I would like to suggest the point of view that it might be
inconsistent for a theory of quantum gravity to require vanishing
torsion on the level of the operators. In analogy to the Gupta–Bleuler
formalism of quantum electrodynamics, it could be possible that this
only is a consistent constraint on the physical states. If this
interpretation was adopted, supersymmetry could be interpreted as a
natural symmetry of the free @xmath gravity theory linking the torsion
degrees of freedom to the vielbein ones. This is my personal motivation
to study supergravity theories from a physical point of view.

I will conclude this chapter with an argument why it might be
interesting to discuss higher dimensional supergravities in order to
quantize the gravitational interaction in @xmath . Taking the
requirement of maximal supersymmetry as a guideline, one is led to
discuss supergravity in @xmath dimensions [ 10 ] . This theory will be
further investigated in chapter 4 .

### 2.1 A brief excursion to quantum electrodynamics

#### 2.1.1 Gauging a symmetry

A physical theory that is quite well understood, adequately tested and
verified by experiment is the classical Dirac theory that describes
fermions such as electrons or quarks in Minkowski space @xmath . As long
as the gravitational interaction can be neglected, it is expected that
the restriction of the manifold to flat Minkowski space is sufficient to
describe experiments.

Since the reason for discussing this topic is of mere motivational
nature, I will stick to the simpler case of electrons, which are
described by Graßmann valued, i.e. anticommuting, sections of the spin
bundle over the manifold @xmath . The corresponding dynamics are

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (2.1.1)

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

have kept the dimension @xmath arbitrary in order to emphasize that the
entire argumentation is independent of the value of @xmath . The @xmath
-matrices fulfill the standard Clifford property ( 6.2.1 ) and @xmath
denotes the Dirac conjugation, i.e. transposition, complex conjugation
and multiplication by @xmath . Due to the latter, the Lagrangian @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (2.1.3)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

The process of transforming a global continuous symmetry into a local

the physical interpretation of this Lagrangian, I want to recapitulate
the recipe for gauging a global symmetry.

1.  Start with a given theory that describes physical objects.

2.  Observe that the corresponding action is invariant under a global
    continuous symmetry group transformation.

3.  Gauge the symmetry by introducing a connection @xmath .

4.  Construct a gauged Lagrangian in such a way that the gauged theory
    is invariant.

#### 2.1.2 Relating the gauged theory to physics

It is obvious from the recipe I provided in the previous section that
the resulting gauged Lagrangian is by no means unique. There are many
mathematically well-defined gauged Lagrangians that could lead to
physical observations. In order to find out which one indeed leads to a
physically interesting theory, one has to establish some correspondence
between an observable object in nature and the gauge field.

For the case of the Dirac theory, this relation is provided by
electromagnetism. The observable electric and magnetic field strengths
@xmath and @xmath are in one-to-one correspondence with the field
strengh @xmath ( 2.1.7 ) in @xmath . As electrons are charged objects,
this identification allows to test if there is a gauged Lagrangian that
corresponds to the interaction of electrons under the electromagnetic
force. It is one of the most exact results in physics that there is a
Lagrangian that fulfills this property, which is provided by the example
I have stated in ( 2.1.8 ).

The gauge field @xmath is usually referred to as the electromagnetic
potential. In contrast to the field strength @xmath , it is not in
one-to-one correspondence to observable objects, because it is only
defined modulo the gauge transformation ( 2.1.5 ). This allows to set
the exact part of the one-form @xmath to zero classically, which implies
@xmath . ² ² 2 In classical electrodynamics, this is called the Lorenz
gauge. This choice is not unique, however: the Coulomb gauge is a
prominent different gauge. This arbitrariness corresponds to the
so-called horizontal polarizations of a photon.

In the introduction to this chapter, I mentioned that I will discuss
quantum aspects at some point. The standard quantization procedure of
the Lagrangian ( 2.1.8 ) has led to remarkable agreement with
experiments [ 54 ] . Therefore, I want to highlight two facts of this
procedure.

1.   The objects to be quantized are the modes parametrizing the space
    of solutions of the free field equation for the gauge potential
    @xmath .

2.   It is inconsistent to impose @xmath as an operator relation. It has
    to be imposed on physical states.

The second point is referred to as the Gupta-Bleuler formalism in
quantum electrodynamics. To phrase this in other words: it appears to be
inconsistent for the quantization of a gauge theory to fix a symmetry
prior to quantization, even though this would not make a difference for
the classical theory. It is important to keep this in mind for the
discussion of gravity in the next section. I close the discussion of
quantum electrodynamics with three comments.

-   I want to emphasize that the definition ( 2.1.4 ) is not a
    restriction, because the explicit form of the Lagrangian ( 2.1.8 )
    was not fixed a priori. Only in this case, the relation ( 2.1.4 )
    should be referred to as minimal coupling. Any non-minimal coupling
    is hence equivalent to choosing a different Lagrangian ( 2.1.8 ) and
    keeping ( 2.1.4 ).

-   For all the other real finite dimensional symmetry Lie groups that
    follow the classification in the sections 1.2.2 and 1.2.4 , the
    procedure works in the same way, keeping in mind the results of
    section 1.4 for more complicated groups. As an example may serve the
    quarks @xmath of quantum chromodynamics that transform as
    non-trivial representations of @xmath ( 1.4.1 ). The connection has
    the form

      -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
         @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (2.1.9)
      -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

    with an action @xmath of the Lie algebra @xmath on the coefficients
    @xmath . The gluons of the strong interaction are set into
    correspondence to the @xmath valued gauge field @xmath .

-   If the gauge group @xmath is not simple, it is convenient to
    introduce gauge fields for all simple parts and ideals separately.
    This is no restriction, because the gauge field always takes values
    in the corresponding Lie algebra @xmath , which is a vector space
    and can hence be decomposed. This will be the case in the next
    section.

### 2.2 Gravity as a gauge theory

All physical theories that are related to observations are formulated in
flat Minkowski space @xmath , unless they involve the gravitational
interaction. This implies that they all have a common external symmetry
group, the Poincaré isometry group @xmath of flat Minkowski space @xmath
( 1.3.21 ). Since all physical fields are expected to couple to gravity,
it is hence natural to apply the gauging recipe provided in section
2.1.1 to the Poincaré group and try to relate the result to the
gravitational interaction afterwards again.

deduced by the standard variational principle. This completes the
gauging procedure of the Poincaré symmetry for any matter theory in
Minkowski space.

#### 2.2.3 General relativity

Einstein proposed in 1915 that any gravitational coupling to a test
particle can be interpreted as a force resulting from an appropriate
change of the coordinate frame. To phrase it differently, for any matter
system there is a coordinate system in which the gravitational coupling
is absent. Any test particle in this frame is referred to as a freely
falling particle. If the torsion tensor @xmath ( 2.2.11a ) is set to
zero, the equations of motion of the action @xmath ( 2.2.15 ) are called
the Einstein equations. This theory is referred to as General Relativity
and it has led to remarkable agreement with experiment.

An important point is that this theory can only be linked to
experiments, if a holonomic or integrable frame is used: these are
equivalent names for a frame @xmath that is induced by coordinates
@xmath . And experiments always measure distances in these coordinates.
This implies that the dynamical object of general relativity is the
metric @xmath ( 1.4.10 ). At every point @xmath , it is given by the
@xmath independent components of a symmetric matrix @xmath . Due to the
associativity of matrix multiplication, the gauge field @xmath of
translations can also be interpreted as a @xmath matrix. ⁶ ⁶ 6 A local
translation acts from the left and a local Lorentz rotation from the
right. Due to associativity, the two actions commute. Since there are no
constraints on this matrix @xmath , the degrees of freedom in equation (
1.4.12 )

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

do not match. I have already addressed this fact in section 1.4.3 : if
the eigenvalues of the vielbein matrix @xmath are positive, then to
every symmetric tensor @xmath there is a unique @xmath orbit @xmath of
vielbein matrices @xmath . ⁷ ⁷ 7 From the gauge theoretic point of view,
the requirement of positive eigenvalues of @xmath is natural due to the
concept of a perturbation expansion @xmath .

The important point is that the Lorentz group, whose orbit is @xmath ,
acts in the same way as the Lorentz subgroup of the Poincaré group that
I have gauged ( 2.2.8 ). This implies that it is consistent to fix any
representative @xmath in the equivalence class @xmath , which is
equivalent to fixing the matrix form of @xmath for all @xmath . Thus, a
local translation @xmath with @xmath induces a compensating local
Lorentz rotation @xmath such that the transformed matrix @xmath has the
same matrix form

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
                                
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

It is this particular constraint of a fixed representative in the
equivalence class @xmath that induces a diffeomorphism action on the
vielbein frame @xmath or in other words, that establishes the link from
the tangent bundle to the spin bundle. Hence, physical fields in the
spin bundle transform under a local translation of coordinates @xmath by
an induced Lorentz action @xmath , which is well-defined because
expectation values always are even in fermions as discussed in section
1.2.5 .

I will adopt this choice ( 2.2.3 ) for the induced Lorentz action of a
diffeomorphism in the sequel. I want to emphasize again that this is a
choice ⁸ ⁸ 8 I am grateful to T. Damour for pointing this out to me.
,but a canonical one, which is due to the following argument.

I have already mentioned in the sections 1.4.3 and 2.2.1 that it would
be consistent to fix the trivial Lorentz action @xmath for the induced
action on the vielbein ( 2.2.3 ). This would imply that the gauge field
@xmath would be invariant under a local translation or a diffeomorphism.
In order to construct an action that is invariant under diffeomorphisms
alone, it would hence be sufficient to consider any polynomial in @xmath
. Thus, even if I imposed vanishing torsion @xmath , the
Einstein–Hilbert action ( 2.2.13 with @xmath ) would not be the unique
possibility of an action @xmath with diffeomorphism invariance any more
that only depends on the vielbein matrix @xmath and is quadratic in
derivatives.

This non-uniqueness is not a contradiction to results from differential
geometry for one reason: the dependence of the action @xmath on @xmath
would not in general be in a way that allows to rephrase @xmath as a
function of the metric @xmath . Requiring this possibility is related to
the symmetry of @xmath or to local Lorentz invariance of the action,
equivalently. And as soon as the entire local Poincaré invariance is
required, the uniqueness property of the Einstein–Hilbert action is
restored, of course. Therefore, it is natural to require that every
diffeomorphism @xmath also induces a local Lorentz rotation @xmath ,
because then it is sufficient to demand diffeomorphism invariance of the
action alone to guarantee the uniqueness property of the
Einstein–Hilbert action.

#### 2.2.4 Geometric interpretation

I have shown that the equations of motion of any theory for which the
Poincaré symmetry is gauged, correspond to a theory that is invariant
under the group of diffeomorphisms @xmath , which is by definition the
group of general coordinate transformations.

Since I defined a local physical theory by its analytic equations of
motion, global properties like the topology of the underlying manifold
do not influence the dynamics. As the gauging procedure is performed in
local coordinates and as the Minkowski space is diffeomorphic to any
open set on any Lorentzian manifold @xmath of equal dimension, I am free
to start with an arbitrarily curved Lorentzian manifold and cover it
with a coordinate chart @xmath containing open sets @xmath . For each
open set @xmath , I can introduce basis vectors @xmath ( 1.1.4 ) that
span @xmath . In the coordinate induced basis of the cotangent space
@xmath , define a Minkowski metric @xmath with respect to which the
ungauged matter Lagrangian is specified. ⁹ ⁹ 9 The metric @xmath will of
course have a different shape in these coordinates in general. Locally,
@xmath is always well-defined, as I have already mentioned in the
context of the conformal isometries ( 1.3.22b ). I am grateful to
D. Giulini for pointing this out to me. Gauging the Poincaré isometry of
@xmath introduces gauge fields @xmath and @xmath . As the gauged
translations are equivalent to coordinate transformations, it is
consistent to identify the gauge field of translations @xmath with the
Lorentzian metric @xmath in this coordinate frame tantamount to the
relation ( 1.4.12 )

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

This procedure can be performed for any open neighbourhood on @xmath and
for any coordinate chart @xmath . The resulting theory is invariant
under gauged translations or diffeomorphisms @xmath -equivalently. The
equations of motion of a local physical theory do not differ for
different manifolds or for different open sets on the same manifold.
They are universal in the sense that the abstract equations of motion
for @xmath are the same for all points @xmath , whereas the metric
tensor @xmath itself is different for different @xmath in general.

For every open set, I could in principle define a separate action in
some coordinate chart that provides these universal equations of motion
after a variation. This is what is actually meant by integrating over a
manifold @xmath . In this spirit, the gauged version of the matter
Lagrangian can be reinterpreted as a local physical theory on an
arbitrary Lorentzian manifold @xmath , whose universal equations of
motion can be derived from the following action in the standard way:

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (2.2.19)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

This action is globally invariant under diffeomorphisms. Therefore,
General Relativity can also be interpreted as follows: given the concept
of a Lorentzian manifold, there is the arbitrariness which coordinate
frame should be chosen to describe a physical theory. General Relativity
claims that the physics is the same in every coordinate chart, i.e. the
equations of motion are universal. Hence, having fixed a coordinate
chart, the resulting equations of motion should be covariant under a
general coordinate transformation, or equivalently under any
diffeomorphism. The subtlety is that thus, non-local diffeomorphisms
@xmath would not be excluded. Hence, although I excluded them in section
1.3.1 , the resulting theory ( 2.2.19 ) is invariant under their action.

To conclude this section, I want to stress that the argumentation from
section 2.1.2 how to relate a gauge theory to physics applies to the
Poincaré group in the same way as to any internal symmetry group. In
particular, the gauged Lagrangian is not uniquely determined by the
gauging procedure. Hence, it is again the experiment that has to fix the
terms that have to be added to the matter Lagrangian, e.g. if higher
curvature corrections have to be added or not. At the moment,
experiments indicate that the Lagrangian @xmath ( 2.2.13 ) is the right
combination ¹⁰ ¹⁰ 10 A different possibility would e.g. be the
untruncated MacDowell–Mansouri action (12) in [ 42 ] , for which there
is however no experimental evidence so far. , in which @xmath is called
the cosmological constant and torsion vanishes @xmath . The latter
constraint is the first example of a consistent constraint that
establishes a relation between the independent gauge fields @xmath and
@xmath ( 2.2.16 ).

#### 2.2.5 Teleparallel interpretation

In his quest for a “unified field theory”, Einstein [ 16 ] has also used
a different interpretation of General Relativity that I briefly want to
sketch. If some coordinate system is fixed, given any metric on an open
set, it is always possible to choose the contorsion tensor @xmath (
2.2.16 ) in the vielbein frame such that the Lorentz connection @xmath
is trivial on this open set:

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (2.2.20)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

This implies that the curvature tensor @xmath ( 2.2.11b ) vanishes. This
statement is not preserved under a change of the coordinate chart or
equivalently a general transformation @xmath , but this is not important
for the evaluation of experiments, because these are evaluated in a
fixed coordinate chart anyway. Given some arbitrary metric, @xmath can
be chosen to be trivial ( 2.2.20 ) implying non-trivial torsion @xmath
in general. Replacing @xmath by @xmath in the Einstein-Hilbert action (
2.2.11b , 2.2.19 ), one obtains a well-defined action that gives rise to
the same dynamics as General Relativity in the geometric interpretation
that I discussed in the previous section. Thus, the constraint of
vanishing curvature is a well-defined procedure locally. It is the
second example of a constraint that links the gauge fields @xmath and
@xmath .

The crucial ingredient to make this interpretation viable is the fixing
of some coordinate system a priori. If the manifold @xmath is
parallelizable, i.e. there is a global coordinate frame, as is the case
for Lie groups and Minkowski space, this procedure is globally
well-defined, too. If this is not the case, it is impossible to satisfy
( 2.2.20 ) on the overlap of two coordinate charts, because ( 2.2.20 )
is not preserved under a change of the coordinate chart with @xmath
being a tensor in contrast to @xmath . This may be the reason why the
teleparallel interpretation of general relativity is often ignored in
the physics literature. ¹¹ ¹¹ 11 A nice historical survey on this theory
can be found in [ 20 ] . Hehl has extended this idea in his work on
metric affine geometry (MAG), in which the affine group @xmath ( 1.3.18
) is gauged. More details and further references can be found in his
review [ 22 ] .

#### 2.2.6 An aspect of quantization

In this section, I would like to push the analogy to quantizing internal
gauge symmetries a bit further. To phrase the content of section 2.2.3
in a different way, physical observations do not exclude that in the
same way as electromagnetic interactions can be interpreted as being
mediated by a non-trivial gauge field @xmath , gravitational
interactions can be interpreted as being mediated by the non-trivial
gauge fields @xmath of the Poincaré group. ¹² ¹² 12 Non-triviality for
the case of @xmath obviously means that the matrix @xmath is different
from the identity matrix.

I have already mentioned at the end of section 2.2.4 that the model that
is usually used to explain present observations assumes vanishing
torsion @xmath and hence explains the gravitational interaction by a
non-vanishing curvature of the universe. The teleparallel interpretation
shows however that it is locally possible to choose a torsion such that
the curvature vanishes without affecting the dynamics of general
relativity. At least locally, there appears to be a symmetry linking the
torsion to the curvature without affecting the dynamics. This
arbitrariness is usually fixed by imposing a constraint on the
connection @xmath ( 2.2.7 ) such that the torsion tensor @xmath (
2.2.11a ) vanishes, what immediately constrains the two gauge fields
@xmath and @xmath in the standard way ( 2.2.16 ) such that all
independent degrees of freedom of @xmath are eliminated.

Next, recall from the discussion of quantizing the electromagnetic
interaction in section 2.1.2 that it was the potential @xmath of the
force that was quantized. This was however only determined up to a gauge
symmetry. The fixing of this symmetry led to the constraint @xmath .
Finally, the argumentation showed that this constraint on the gauge
field must be imposed on the physical states after having performed the
quantization process, and not prior to it. Hence, if there was any
symmetry relating the torsion to the vielbein, it could as well turn out
to be inconsistent to impose vanishing torsion prior to quantization.

Therefore, I do not see any mathematical argument why one should not
quantize the entire Einstein-Cartan theory, which is described by
gauging the Poincaré group without the constraint of vanishing torsion
being fixed on the operator level. For the physical states however, this
constraint could be enforced in the same way as it is the case for the
Lorenz gauge in quantum electrodynamics (Gupta-Bleuler formalism). ¹³ ¹³
13 This analogy is not compelling, of course. Fixing the Lorenz gauge
@xmath in electrodynamics breaks the local @xmath gauge symmetry,
whereas fixing the torsion @xmath does not break the local Poincaré
symmetry. Only the additional symmetry linking the torsion to the
vielbein is broken.

It turns out that in @xmath , a symmetry indeed exists that relates the
torsion to the metric. Since I have restricted the class of manifolds
under consideration to manifolds with vanishing first and second
Stiefel–Whitney class [ 41 , 45 ] , it is possible to define a Graßmann
valued section @xmath of the tensor product of the cotangent bundle with
the spin bundle. By the following identification, the field @xmath
contains degrees of freedom of the torsion tensor

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (2.2.21)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

The coefficients @xmath of the section @xmath are denoted with respect
to the vielbein frame ( 2.2.4 ) of the cotangent space. The matrices
@xmath are representations of the Clifford algebra ( 6.2.1 ). In @xmath
, there is a Majorana representation of the Clifford algebra. Hence, the
matrices @xmath are real objects, which is the reason why no complex
conjugation is involved in the definition ( 2.2.21 ). The spinor indices
of @xmath are kept implicit as in section 2.1.1 .

Since the torsion @xmath transforms as a Lorentz tensor under local
Poincaré transformations, the section @xmath also transforms as a tensor
modulo @xmath , following the discussion from section 1.2.5 . Hence any
Lorentz scalar that is bilinear in @xmath , is invariant under the
diffeomorphism group @xmath .

I want to construct a Lorentz scalar @xmath from the torsion degrees of
freedom encoded in @xmath . The standard choice is a Rarita–Schwinger

The physical theory corresponding to the Lagrangian @xmath ( 2.2.13 )

The infinitesimal variation @xmath defines a physical symmetry in the

acts on. Therefore, it is not a gauged version of a global symmetry
algebra or superalgebra.

Chapter 4 will deal with a gravitational theory that is defined by
requiring maximal supersymmetry. Before presenting further details on
this theory in section 2.3 , I want to close this part with some
comments.

-   It is obvious from the definition of the gravitino @xmath ( 2.2.21 )
    that not all @xmath degrees of freedom of the torsion tensor @xmath
    can be covered by the @xmath degrees of freedom of @xmath . However,
    the vielbein @xmath in @xmath also has @xmath degrees of freedom
    prior to imposing the equations of motion and fixing the
    representative in the Lorentz orbit @xmath . Hence, supersymmetry
    appears to be the maximal symmetry that can be introduced to link
    the torsion tensor @xmath to the vielbein @xmath . ¹⁵ ¹⁵ 15 The
    degrees of freedom after imposing the equations of motion and fixing
    the gauges also coincide, of course, which follows from the closure
    of the supersymmetry algebra on the solutions @xmath and @xmath of
    the equations of motion.

-   The @xmath @xmath supergravity [ 17 ] provided by the Lagrangian
    @xmath ( 2.2.13 ) with ( 2.2.21 , 2.2.22 ) and @xmath possibly
    describes the free gravitational interaction that should be
    quantized. For the construction of propagators in such a quantum
    gravity, it is not important if a matter coupling to the system or
    @xmath breaks the supersymmetry or not. This is in analogy to the
    conformal symmetry of the free Maxwell system that is quantized in
    quantum electrodynamics: the vertices break the conformal symmetry
    due to the coupling to massive Dirac fermions, but not the
    propagators of the Maxwell field. I want to emphasize that in the
    present construction, neither the matter system has to be
    supersymmetric as suggested in [ 17 ] , nor that the Lorentzian
    manifold is elevated to some supermanifold. This would lead to a
    different theory [ 31 , 52 ] .

-   Since the connection @xmath ( 2.2.7 ) is Poincaré algebra valued and
    not @xmath valued, I refrain from calling the diffeomorphism group
    @xmath the gauge group of general relativity. This distinction is
    important for the discussion of Goldstone bosons in the context of
    non-linear realizations in section 3.3.4 , because with this
    interpretation, there only are finitely many Goldstone bosons in
    general relativity [ 3 ] , and not infinitely many [ 4 ] .

-   In the introduction to this chapter, I have already mentioned that
    the description of a physical force as a gauge theory offers the
    possibility to use the concept of a perturbation expansion in a
    coupling constant in its domain of validity to compare to
    experiments. For the gravitational interaction, the standard
    approach is an expansion of the gauge fields @xmath about the
    trivial case @xmath . Due to the semisimplicity of the Poincaré
    group, there is only one coupling constant for both gauge fields [ 9
    ] .

#### 2.2.7 Comments on the literature

Einstein orginally phrased General Relativity in the coordinate induced
frame @xmath , and not in the one induced by the repère mobile or
vielbein @xmath ( 1.4.10 ). The first interpretation of the
gravitational interaction as a gauge group is due to Utiyama [ 61 ] in
1954, who only gauged the Lorentz subgroup of the Poincaré group: in
order to obtain the dynamics of General Relativity he had to impose the
symmetry of the Christoffel symbols by hand. Sciama and Kibble [ 60 ]
gauged the entire Poincaré group in 1961, which yields an
Einstein-Cartan theory. ¹⁶ ¹⁶ 16 In 1976, Cho [ 9 ] claimed to have
discovered an alternative approach to describe General Relativity as a
gauge theory, by not identifying the translation operator with the
derivative operator a priori and gauging the subgroup of translations
alone. However, he requires the resulting theory to be independent of
the particular coordinate frame chosen. This becomes clearer in Hehl’s
review [ 22 ] , where it is referred to as “soft Lorentz covariance”. In
both cases, this is an additional constraint that is not provided by the
gauge theory, as long as not the entire Poincaré group is gauged.
However, I am not aware of any proposal in the literature that
elaborates a possibility to link the gauge fields @xmath and @xmath of
the two subgroups by a (super)symmetry relation.

### 2.3 Maximal supergravity

#### 2.3.1 Quantization, higher dimensions and more supersymmetry

In section 2.2.6 , I have argued that supersymmetry may be interesting
for the quantization of the gravitational interaction in @xmath
dimensions: as soon as one local coordinate system is fixed, there is a
symmetry transformation in these coordinates that generates torsion from
the metric. Hence, it is possible that there is a symmetry action that
maps curvature to torsion and vice versa in the fixed coordinate system.
Global aspects of the manifold are by definition not important for the
local physical theory which is why a restriction to parallizable
manifolds is not compulsory. As for the discussion of quantum
electrodynamics from section 2.1.2 , it is the experiment that will have
to decide if this interpretation with non-trivial torsion degrees of
freedom is relevant for a quantum theory of gravity or not.

In order to make contact to experiments, it looks promising to follow
the analogy to quantum electrodynamics, i.e. to compute expectation
values of a quantum theory of gravity by evaluating Feynman graphs.
These consist of vertices and propagators. I want to focus on the
propagators. In quantum electrodynamics, these correspond to the
solutions in the zero coupling limit, which is the free field theory,
described by the wave equation whose space of solutions is parametrized
by the Fourier decomposition.

In the case of gravity, the free field theory is described by the
Einstein equation, for which a parametrization of the space of solutions
is problematic. ¹⁷ ¹⁷ 17 Of course, one could formally introduce an
expansion @xmath and solve the wave equation for @xmath . This is
referred to as a gravitational wave. It would define a propagator for
the linearized theory, and the infinitely many terms of higher
polynomial order in @xmath could then be considered as interaction
terms. This is the way quantum chromodynamics is handled, but there are
only finitely many interaction terms in this case. One reason for this
is that these differential equations are weakly hyperbolic, which is
related to the diffeomorphism invariance of the theory.

The problem one is confronted with is similar to the one Perelman solved
in proving the Poincaré conjecture [ 53 ] . For this, Hamilton’s concept
of a Ricci flow was of crucial importance. I will not review it at this
point, but I content myself with stating that this is a one parameter
family of @xmath -dimensional metrics fulfilling a weakly parabolic
differential equation in @xmath dimensions. It is possible to establish
a correspondence between @xmath -dimensional diffeomorphisms and @xmath
-dimensional ones fulfilling a partial differential equation. This trick
of a lift to a higher dimension allows to modify the dynamics to be
strictly parabolic and hence solvable by introducing an additional
scalar field @xmath . ¹⁸ ¹⁸ 18 I am grateful to G. Huisken for
explaining the details.

Although Perelman only discussed particular diffeomorphisms in @xmath
and parabolic systems, the technical procedure could still be
interesting for the present discussion. Perelman mentioned in [ 53 ]
that the scalar field @xmath exactly is the dilaton used in string
theories. From a gravitational point of view, this results from a
reduction of a higher dimensional pure gravity theory on a torus à la
Kaluza–Klein. Thus, the scalar field can be interpreted as a part of a
metric in a higher dimensional space. I would like to suggest that this
procedure of “gauge fixing the diffeomorphism symmetry” by adding more
dimensions and thus more fields could also work for the Einstein
equation in @xmath , to transform them into strictly hyperbolic
equations. This programme does of course not tell in which way the
higher dimensional theory should be stated.

In this thesis, supersymmetry will serve as a guideline to construct a
candidate for such a theory. It is well known that the maximal
supergravity theory in @xmath has @xmath linearly independent
supersymmetry variations. These link additional bosonic and fermionic
degrees of freedom to the vielbein @xmath and the gravitino @xmath from
section 2.2.6 . That @xmath is indeed the maximal number of
supersymmetries results from a perturbative argument: given a theory
with more than @xmath supersymmetries in @xmath , consider the
linearized theory about Minkowksi background and constant transformation
parameter @xmath . In this setting, the supersymmetry algebra ( 2.2.24 )
reduces to the Super-Poincaré algebra with well-defined, spin valued
supercharges @xmath that result from splitting the constant parameter
@xmath from the supersymmetry variation @xmath ( 2.2.23 ) in complete
analogy to extracting the generators @xmath from the vector field in (
1.3.14 ). Hence, all fields of this theory build up a supermultiplet of
this superalgebra and for @xmath , the highest spin of the fields in
this supermultiplet is greater than @xmath . For this linearized theory,
the argumentation from [ 46 ] is valid that rules out supergravity
theories with @xmath . ¹⁹ ¹⁹ 19 I want to emphasize that this concept of
a Super-Poincaré algebra fails as soon as the restriction to the
linearized limit is lifted, which is obvious from equation ( 2.2.24 ).
Strictly speaking, supercharges @xmath as operators mapping fields to
fields are ill-defined in the non-linear domain of supergravity
theories.

This is my personal motivation for studying @xmath @xmath supergravity
and its possible relation to the gravitational interaction in @xmath
dimensions. The additional fields of @xmath supergravity would hence be
interpreted as some kind of Lagrangian multiplyers of the diffeomorphism
symmetry and not as measurable excitations. In this context, it is also
interesting to observe that @xmath supergravity is much better behaved
in a perturbation expansion than was originally expected and may even be
ultraviolet finite in perturbation theory [ 2 ] .

I have suggested that adding more dimensions might help to improve the
analytic behaviour of the Einstein equation in analogy to the discussion
of the Ricci flow. The additional bosonic fields would follow from a
higher dimensional vielbein by a reduction à la Kaluza–Klein. I will
partly prove in chapter 4 that this is indeed the case for @xmath @xmath
supergravity: the bosonic fields can be combined in a @xmath
-dimensional vielbein, if the transition functions @xmath are restricted
in a quite peculiar way. An intermediate step towards this goal is
provided by @xmath @xmath supergravity [ 10 ] that I will discuss next.

#### 2.3.2 Supergravity in @xmath

A result of major importance for this thesis is due to Cremmer, Julia
and Scherk [ 10 ] . They have shown that all solutions of @xmath @xmath
supergravity are the solutions of @xmath @xmath supergravity with @xmath
commuting Killing vectors. This is equivalent to stating that @xmath
@xmath can be constructed from @xmath @xmath by a reduction à la
Kaluza–Klein on a @xmath -dimensional hypertorus @xmath . This does not
falsify my claim that it can also be derived from a @xmath -dimensional
theory, because @xmath supergravity has an additional bosonic degree of
freedom apart from the vielbein @xmath , a three-form potential @xmath
which is only defined modulo the gauge transformation

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (2.3.1)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

with an arbitrary two-form @xmath . I am using the same letter @xmath as
for the electromagnetic potential, because the gauge transformation is

The guideline how to restrict the sixty-dimensional geometry is provided

#### 2.3.4 Hidden symmetries

I have already mentioned that the guideline how to restrict the @xmath
-dimensional geometry is provided by the hidden symmetries of @xmath
@xmath supergravity that are global internal symmetries. They result
from a reduction à la Kaluza–Klein on a flat spacelike hypertorus @xmath
. I want to sketch briefly that this is equivalent to discussing the
subspace of solutions of @xmath supergravity with @xmath independent
commuting spacelike Killing vectors.

A reduction à la Kaluza–Klein on a flat spacelike hypertorus @xmath is
based on the statement that @xmath endowed with the flat Euclidean
metric @xmath can be embedded in the @xmath -dimensional Lorentzian
manifold @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (2.3.6)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

using the same coordinate chart on both sides. Since I do not discuss
non-local aspects in this thesis, it is sufficient to show that the
tangent space @xmath for every @xmath allows for this embedding, because
locally, the manifold is diffeomorphic to the tangent space. Next recall
from the sections 1.3.3 and 1.4.3 that the metric is flat if and only if
the coordinate induced frame and the vielbein frame coincide and that
this is the case if the coordinate induced basis vectors of the tangent
space are independent Killing vectors. As coordinate induced basis
vectors commute by definition, the presence of @xmath independent
commuting spacelike Killing vectors restricts the possible metrics
@xmath to the ones that allow for this embedding ( 2.3.6 ), which proves
the equivalence.∎

For a gravitational theory, the arbitrariness of the coordinate chart is
encoded in the diffeomorphism symmetry. Having fixed a coordinate chart,
the internal symmetry @xmath links one solution of the theory to another
one. Given one solution that fulfills the constraint ( 2.3.6 ), it is
obvious that a general diffeomorphism would not map it to another one
that fulfills ( 2.3.6 ). Therefore, only a subgroup of @xmath is a
symmetry of the space of solutions of @xmath supergravity with @xmath
commuting Killing vectors.

The interpretation with @xmath commuting Killing vectors shows that the
group of general coordinate transformations is restricted to @xmath : in
order to preserve the property of commutativity of Killing vector
fields, the Jacobian matrix @xmath of the diffeomorphism @xmath must be
constant. And I have shown in the sections 1.3.2 and 1.3.3 that this is
the characterizing property of the affine group @xmath ( 1.3.18 ).

The symmetry group @xmath hence is the internal symmetry of the space of
solutions of @xmath supergravity with @xmath independent commuting
Killing spinors. Performing a Fourier expansion in the coordinates of
the hypertorus @xmath and neglecting all non-trivial modes is known as a
compactification on @xmath . This procedure transforms the external
symmetry group @xmath of an @xmath -dimensional theory into an external
part @xmath and a global internal part @xmath of an @xmath -dimensional
theory. In accordance with the definitions of global internal symmetries
from section 1.1.2 , @xmath maps solutions to solutions without
affecting the coordinates. It is however obvious from the constraint (
2.3.6 ) that not all affine linear diffeomorphisms @xmath map a given
solution to a different one, because the isometry subgroup @xmath of
@xmath will not affect a given solution. This implies that the @xmath
scalars of the @xmath -dimensional theory resulting from the
eleven-dimensional metric, are parametrized by the coset

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

For @xmath supergravity, this global symmetry @xmath is enlarged by the
degrees of freedom of the three-form potential @xmath to @xmath . ²¹ ²¹
21 For @xmath , the @xmath -dimensional @xmath tensor is used to combine
the bosonic degrees of freedom in the appropriate representation of
@xmath . The fermions transform as representations of the universal
