I want to conclude this review with the remark that searching for higher
dimensional supergravity theories that give rise to a coset model for a
given semi-simple group @xmath is called group-disintegration or
oxidation, because it is the inverse process to reduction [ 12 , 34 , 37
] . @xmath supergravity is commonly referred to as the “oxidation end
point” of @xmath @xmath supergravity which is due to the following
argument. If the diffeomorphism symmetry @xmath is not restricted, the
parameter @xmath of the supersymmetry variation transforms as a spin
representation of @xmath . For maximal supergravity with @xmath real,
four component spinors in @xmath this leads to the restriction @xmath ,
because for any higher @xmath , the dimension of the irreducible spin
representation would be greater than @xmath . However, this restriction
can be circumvented if the diffeomorphism symmetry @xmath is restricted
in such a way that the induced action is only by a proper subgroup of
@xmath . Chapter 4 will provide an example with @xmath .

#### 2.3.5 Hidden symmetries in @xmath

It is still an up to date question in string theory to look for
consistent compactifications of @xmath supergravity to @xmath physical
dimensions. However, not on all manifolds with the correct dimensions,
compactifications are admissible [ 55 ] . Hence, @xmath supergravity
must possess some information about preferred manifolds on which a
compactification is possible. In proving the consistency of a
compactification on the sphere @xmath , de Wit and Nicolai showed that
there is indeed some remnant of a hidden symmetry in the
non-compactified theory:

@xmath supergravity has a local @xmath -symmetry [ 68 ] .

Guided by the @xmath gauged supergravity [ 11 ] , their ansatz reduced
the local Lorentz symmetry @xmath to @xmath . Then, in order to discuss
a linear problem, they focussed on the supersymmetry variations. They
were able to write these in a @xmath -covariant form in @xmath by
combining the degrees of freedom of the vielbein @xmath and the
three-form potential @xmath into @xmath representations with an even
number of @xmath indices. Finally, they proved that this covariance can
be extended to the equations of motion of @xmath supergravity. ²³ ²³ 23
There is no similar statement for @xmath up to now, because Nicolai did
not show the final step in [ 47 ] , which is the covariance of the
equations of motion under @xmath . There is no similar result for @xmath
either, whereas @xmath -covariance trivially follows from @xmath [ 69 ]
.

The @xmath -covariance serves as the starting point for my investigation
of a @xmath -dimensional exceptional geometry in chapter 4 . Focussing
on the bosons, the covariance group of the equations of motion of @xmath
supergravity is @xmath , which I have shown in section 1.2.5 to be the
maximal compact subgroup of @xmath . This nourishes the idea that a
theory that is invariant under an @xmath action on the coordinates may
have the same field content as @xmath supergravity. Representation
theory then fixes

  -- -------- --
     @xmath   
  -- -------- --

as the lowest possible dimension in which such a theory can be defined.
In chapter 4 , I will explicitly show that for a @xmath -dimensional
subsector of @xmath supergravity, the supersymmetry variations ( 2.3.4 )
can be consistently lifted to relations in @xmath dimensions, whose
reduction to @xmath dimensions does not introduce additional degrees of
freedom and that are strongly constrained by requiring an external
@xmath -symmetry. In constructing this theory, I will use the concept of
non-linear realizations, which will be the topic of the next chapter.

## Chapter 3 Non-linear realizations

The aim of this thesis is to construct a sixty-dimensional exceptional
geometry whose @xmath -dimensional vielbein exactly comprises the
bosonic fields of @xmath @xmath supergravity. The exceptional geometry
corresponds to the restriction of the symmetry group @xmath to a
subgroup that respects the fact that a @xmath -dimensional subvielbein
@xmath of the @xmath -dimensional vielbein is an @xmath matrix. The main
result of chapter 4 will be that it is possible to construct @xmath
-covariant supersymmetry variations that are the ones of @xmath
supergravity ( 2.3.4 ) after a compactification of @xmath dimensions
without adding further bosonic or fermionic fields, if the bosonic
degrees of freedom are restricted to the ones described by the
subvielbein @xmath and if only the corresponding @xmath dimensions are
taken into account.

The crucial ingredient for this to be consistent at all is the
restriction of the group of general coordinate transformations @xmath to
a subgroup. An adequate description of this procedure is provided by the
theory of non-linear realizations, which I briefly want to motivate.

I have shown in chapter 2 that the connection @xmath is the basic
building block of a gravitational theory. In particular, @xmath is
sufficient to construct the Lagrangian @xmath ( 2.2.13 ) of general
relativity. A result of section 2.2.1 was that considering the vielbein
@xmath as a gauge field implies that the Lagrangian may only depend on
it through gauge covariant Lorentz tensors like torsion or curvature (
2.2.11 ). A corollary to this statement is that there does not exist a
tensor that is of first order in derivatives and that only depends on
the vielbein degrees of freedom, if full @xmath -invariance for a theory
is required. This is in particular true for a torsion tensor. Hence, a
connection @xmath with @xmath -equivariance is uniquely determined to be
the Levi–Civita connection ( 2.2.16 ) modulo a torsion tensor that
cannot be linear in derivatives, if it exclusively depends on vielbein
degrees of freedom - a statement that I will prove explicitly in this
chapter.

Keeping in mind the necessity for the exceptional geometry to restrict
the diffeomorphism group @xmath to a subgroup, this chapter will deal
with the implications of such a restriction to this uniqueness result
for the connection @xmath .

I will start by constructing the general connection @xmath with
equivariance under the affine subgroup @xmath ( 1.3.18 ) of the
diffeomorphism group @xmath . In section 3.2 , I will prove that
requiring simultaneous equivariance under the abelian group @xmath of
conformal diffeomorphisms ( 1.3.26 ) restores the uniqueness result for
the connection @xmath . This has become known as the procedure of
Borisov & Ogievetsky [ 3 ] .

A guideline for the proof is provided by Ogievetsky’s theorem that I
have quoted in section 1.3.4 : any analytic vector field @xmath is in
the closure of the vector space generated by the conformal Killing
vector field of Minkowski space @xmath ( 1.3.22b ), the affine vector
field @xmath ( 1.3.11 ) and multiple commutators thereof.

I will conclude this chapter with highlighting the differences of my
presentation to the orginal one by Borisov and Ogievetsky [ 3 ] and to
related work in the literature [ 29 , 39 , 58 ] . I will also explain
why I do not share their original motivation to consider the
gravitational interaction on the same footing as internal symmetries in
quantum field theory, to quote Ogievetsky [ 51 ] :
“In deep analogy to the fact that pions are connected with non-linear
realizations of the dynamical chiral @xmath symmetry (see, e.g. [ 63 ] )
gravity field proves to be connected with common non-linear realizations
of the dynamical conformal and affine symmetries [ 29 ] .”

### 3.1 Connections with affine linear equivariance

In section 1.3.3 , I have defined the Poincaré group by the subgroup of

Since I am only interested in local properties of a theory in this

this chapter. ¹ ¹ 1 Due to the Lie property, every subgroup of @xmath
can be defined by the algebraic approach, which is not obvious for the

#### 3.1.4 Connections with affine linear equivariance

In this section, I will determine the possible dependences of
connections @xmath with affine linear equivariance on the vielbein
@xmath . At the end of section 3.1.2 , I have already argued that every
connection that is built from the degrees of freedom of the affine coset
@xmath ( 3.1.6 ) provides an admissible choice. This is in particular
true for the minimal connection @xmath ( 3.1.21 ). The statement that
two connections may only differ by a tensor establishes the link between
a general connection @xmath with affine linear equivariance and @xmath :

###### Proposition 3.1

Let @xmath be a physical field in a representation of @xmath on which a
symmetry may act. Then any two connections @xmath and @xmath on @xmath
that are equivariant under this symmetry transformation may only differ
by a Lorentz tensor @xmath that is covariant with respect to the
symmetry.

###### Proof.

The proof starts by writing twice the definition of a connection on the
coefficients @xmath of a physical field following from section 1.4

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

To simplify the notation, I have not fixed a basis of the cotangent
space and included the one-forms @xmath in the objects instead. To
complete the proof, I have to show that the one-form valued object

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (3.1.22)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

is covariant. In other words, @xmath has to transform as a Lorentz
tensor under the action induced by the symmetry. This is true, because
the definition of equivariance under a symmetry transformation from
section 1.1.3 implies that @xmath is covariant with respect to it.
Therefore, the difference

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

also is covariant and @xmath transforms as a Lorentz tensor under this
symmetry transformation.∎

The symmetry under consideration is the restriction of @xmath to its
affine subgroup @xmath ( 1.3.18 ). Thus, any connection @xmath with

@xmath ( 3.1.21 ) by a Lorentz tensor @xmath . Using the frame ( 3.1.18

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (3.1.23)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

The class of all connections with affine linear equivariance is hence

diffeomorphisms @xmath . It will be important for the construction of

###### Theorem 3.2

Given locality of the theory, a Lorentz tensor @xmath that is covariant
with respect to the affine subgroup @xmath ( 1.3.18 ) of the
diffeomorphisms may only depend on the vielbein @xmath implicitly:

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (3.1.24)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ----------

@xmath is defined in ( 3.1.20a ) and the second dependence is to be
understood in the following way: @xmath may depend on arbitrary powers
of the minimal connection @xmath ( 3.1.21 ) acting on arbitrary Lorentz
tensors that do not have to be constructed from the vielbein alone.

The tensors @xmath that are of first order in derivatives, will be of
particular importance for supergravity in the context of exceptional
geometry which is the topic of chapter 4 . It follows from theorem 3.2
that these tensors must be linear in @xmath ( 3.1.20a ). From a purely
group theoretic point of view however, there is no reason to restrict to
tensors @xmath that are linear in derivatives.

Since the affine group @xmath ( 1.3.18 ) is a subgroup of the
diffeomorphisms @xmath , all connections with @xmath -equivariance are
of the form ( 3.1.23 ). The converse is not true, however: it is obvious
that not all connections of the form ( 3.1.23 ) are @xmath -equivariant.
Requiring affine linear equivariance is only a necessary condition for
@xmath -equivariance. By Ogievetsky’s theorem from section 1.3.4 , the
necessary and sufficient condition should be to demand simultaneous

1.3.26 ). I will explicitly show this in the next section.

### 3.2 Connections with @xmath-equivariance

The proof of Ogievetsky’s theorem and the subsequent discussion from

I will follow the same procedure as in the preceding section. I start
with introducing some conventions for the diffeomorphism under

##### The inverse Higgs effect

#### 3.2.6 Summary and corollaries

Following the procedure pioneered by Borisov & Ogievetsky, I started by
constructing the general class of connections with affine linear
equivariance ( 3.1.23 ). This was completely independent of the explicit
matrix form of the vielbein @xmath . In extracting the connections
@xmath with conformal equivariance from this class, I fixed the matrix
form of the vielbein to be symmetric. This does not imply a priori that
these @xmath are equivariant under conformal diffeomorphisms @xmath for
a different Lorentz gauge choice, too. However, since the result of the
procedure is the same as the manifestly gauge invariant connection
defined in ( 2.2.16 ), the @xmath -equivariance of @xmath is independent
of the choice of the Lorentz gauge. To sum up, the fixing of the
vielbein gauge was a technical procedure to construct this connection
@xmath . The result is independent of the vielbein gauge and @xmath
-equivariant.

A corollary to theorem 3.2 is the statement that the contorsion tensor
@xmath ( 3.2.23 ) cannot be linear in derivatives, if it exclusively
depends on vielbein degrees of freedom: requiring conformal covariance
rules out linear terms in @xmath , which is obvious from equation (
3.2.19 ). I will show in chapter 4 that this is not true any more, if
the symmetry group @xmath is restricted to a subgroup as in the case
appropriate for the discussion of @xmath supergravity.

In the context of this exceptional geometry, the restriction of the
conformal diffeomorphisms to a @xmath -dimensional submanifold with
@xmath will be important. Where local questions are concerned, it is
sufficient to discuss a @xmath -dimensional open subset @xmath of the
open set @xmath , on which the conformal diffeomorphisms are
well-defined as explained in section 1.3.2 . Then, the corollary 1.11 to
Ogievetsky’s theorem from section 1.3.4 is applicable. It implies that
any connection @xmath with affine linear equivariance in @xmath
dimensions and conformal equivariance in @xmath dimensions must be
@xmath -equivariant.

### 3.3 Other perspectives

The necessity of the fixing of the Lorentz gauge for the vielbein matrix
@xmath for the construction à la Borisov & Ogievetsky may appear
unnatural from a geometric point of view. Insisting on manifest Lorentz
gauge covariance throughout the construction leads to a unified coset
description of the diffeomeorphism group @xmath . Its minimal connection
is the Levi–Civita connection modulo a class of specific torsion
tensors. Since the torsion tensor of supergravity theories ( 2.2.21 ) is
not covered by this class, this description appears to be inappropriate
for the discussion of supergravity, however.

After reviewing this unified coset description in the first section, I
will comment on the original interpretation of Borisov & Ogievetsky [ 3
] motivated from the discussion of internal symmetries. In particular, I
will argue that the notion of Goldstone bosons may not be appropriate
for this setting.

I will conclude with a brief survey of West’s @xmath conjecture that
will serve as a guiding principle for the construction of the
exceptional geometry of supergravity in chapter 4 .

#### 3.3.1 A unified coset description

To start with, I want to emphasize that I only used the affine coset and
the conformal one as auxiliary objects in order to construct minimal
connections @xmath ( 3.1.21 ) and @xmath ( 3.2.15 ). These proved useful
to determine the general dependence of the @xmath -equivariant
connection @xmath on the vielbein @xmath . In particular, a dependence
of @xmath on an arbitrary torsion tensor @xmath , which is not part of
any coset, cannot be excluded.

A different approach would be to consider the coset as a fundamental
object and to insist on manifest Lorentz covariance throughout the
procedure of Borisov & Ogievetsky. A quick look at the discussion in
section 3.2.3 shows that the restriction to the symmetric gauge choice
for the vielbein matrix is only due to the absence of the unimodular
vielbein in the conformal coset @xmath ( 3.2.8 ). A global left action
by @xmath on the modified coset

  -- -------- --
     @xmath   
  -- -------- --

would formally lead to the same compensating Lorentz rotation @xmath as
a conformal diffeomorphism @xmath for any gauge choice of the vielbein (
3.2.3 ). Ogievetsky’s theorem 1.10 implies however that this modified
coset does not close under a left action with all generators: it has to
be enlarged to a parametrization of the entire diffeomorphism group
coset @xmath . This introduces infinitely many new parameters for the
generators @xmath of @xmath with their representation @xmath as vector
fields ( 1.3.37 )

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (3.3.1)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

The generalized coset would hence be of the form

#### 3.3.2 What about torsion?

Since torsion is a possible part of the connection, it should be
included in the coset @xmath in such a way that it affects the minimal
connection. The equation ( 3.3.4 ) shows that it can only be a part of
the coefficient @xmath along the generator of degree @xmath . As torsion
is a tensor by definition, it would only change the tensorial part of
@xmath . As @xmath is not a tensor under @xmath , it is obvious from
equation ( 3.3.5 ) that possible torsion in this setting corresponds to
a non-vanishing @xmath . If I do not set @xmath , I can still substitute
( 3.3.5 ) in the formula for minimal connection @xmath ( 3.3.4 ). The
general ansatz for the coefficient

  -- -------- -------- -- --
     @xmath   @xmath      
  -- -------- -------- -- --

     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (3.3.6)
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

This is however not a general expression for torsion, because it cannot
be inverted: not for any @xmath exists an @xmath such that this equation
holds. This is obvious from observing that any contorsion tensor of the
form ( 3.3.6 ) fulfills the equation

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

which is not true for a general contorsion tensor. In particular, the
bifermionic torsion of @xmath supergravity ( 2.2.21 ) does not fulfill
this constraint. I want to remark that Kirsch did not discuss any
non-vanishing torsion in [ 39 ] . ¹⁴ ¹⁴ 14 He only conjectured that
non-vanishing torsion might be connected to a non-commutativity of the
space-time coordinates. Hence, this result exceeds his work.

To sum up, the unified coset model @xmath provides a nice way to discuss
@xmath -equivariant connections with a class of particular torsion
terms. It is not general however. As the torsion of supergravity is not
in this class, I will henceforth work with my interpretation that
considers the two finite dimensional cosets as auxiliary objects and not
as fundamental ones.

Concerning torsion, a comment on Borisov & Ogievetsky’s original
presentation [ 3 ] also is necessary. I proved in section 3.2.6 that the
contorsion tensor @xmath ( 3.2.23 ) cannot be linear in derivatives, if
it exclusively depends on vielbein degrees of freedom. An example for
@xmath of third order in derivatives is provided by @xmath with the
Riemann tensor defined in ( 2.2.11b ). Only an additional constraint,
such as a restriction on the power of derivatives, may exclude such
torsion terms. Since Borisov and Ogievetsky did not state anything like
that, their ansatz, eq. (28) in [ 3 ] , is incomplete. This is the
reason why they claim to obtain general relativity from @xmath
-covariance alone, without demanding vanishing torsion explicitly.

Torsion also is very interesting from another point of view: the
requirement of vanishing torsion for the general connection @xmath with
affine linear equivariance ( 3.1.23 ) uniquely singles out the Levi
Civita connection, without requiring the equivariance under conformal
diffeomorphism @xmath at all. ¹⁵ ¹⁵ 15 To see this, observe that the
non-vanishing torsion of the minimal connection @xmath ( 3.1.21 ) is
proportional to @xmath ( 3.1.20a ), which I show at the end of section
6.3.1 in the appendix.

#### 3.3.3 Goldstone bosons for internal symmetry groups

It’s obvious from the introduction to this chapter that Borisov &
Ogievetsky’s procedure [ 3 ] was strongly inspired by the theory of
non-linear realizations of internal symmetries [ 29 , 57 ] . In
particular, they linked their construction to the Goldstone phenomenon.
In this section that closely follows Salam’s and Strathdee’s original
work [ 57 ] , I want to recapitulate the original idea. In the following
section 3.3.4 , I will point out the problems with an extension of the
Goldstone phenomenon to external symmetries because of which I am not
going to follow this line of argumentation in this thesis.

To understand what is meant by a Goldstone boson, one first has to
recall the definition of a spontaneously broken symmetry @xmath , which
is a symmetry of the Lagrangian, but not of the ground state of a
quantum system. In order to translate this into the language used in my
thesis, I want to quote the example from [ 57 ] :

Consider the chiral symmetry group @xmath for @xmath . The chiral
Lagrangian contains pions @xmath only in the form @xmath . Therefore,
the spin-zero particles @xmath are massless. Hence, a vacuum state is
indistinguishable from a state with any even number of zero frequency
pions forming a representation of @xmath with isospin label @xmath . In
other words, the state with lowest energy (which is the ground state) is
physically degenerate. Although the chiral Lagrangian is invariant under
a @xmath -action, the triplet of pions @xmath does not form a
representation of @xmath . It only parametrizes the coset @xmath , which
implies that a @xmath -action on the pions is realized non-linearly. The
degeneracy of the ground state together with the non-linear realization
of the symmetry @xmath leads to a spontaneous break-down of the chiral
symmetry @xmath . The pions are the Goldstone bosons in this example.

Salam and Strathdee [ 57 ] generalized this procedure to arbitrary
groups @xmath . They introduced the notion of a preferred field. This is
a field that transforms in a constrained way under the action of a
global symmetry group @xmath . The particles associated to these
preferred fields are Goldstone bosons [ 21 ] .

Following Wigner’s interpretation of the momentum four-vector as a
Lorentz boost [ 67 ] , they identified these preferred fields as
particular components of a coset @xmath , the so-called reducing matrix.
The action of an element @xmath of the symmetry group @xmath on the
coset @xmath is then defined by a multiplication from the left by @xmath
and a right multiplication with an element of the subgroup @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (3.3.7)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

The latter is fixed by requiring that the transformed reducing matrix
@xmath or equivalently, the transformed preferred fields, again satisfy
the constraint imposed on them. Thus, @xmath depends on @xmath in
general which implies a non-linear transformation of the preferred
fields under @xmath . This constraint is nothing but the choice which
representative in the @xmath orbit of @xmath has been fixed.

Let @xmath and @xmath denote the Lie algebras corresponding to the Lie
groups @xmath and @xmath . Then the projection of the Maurer–Cartan form
@xmath on @xmath is invariant under a global left action ( 3.3.7 ).
Since the preferred fields parametrize the coset @xmath , the
coefficient of @xmath along the vectors spanning @xmath defines a
derivative of the preferred field, a “covariant derivative”.

The quotation marks for “covariant derivative” were introduced in [ 29 ]
, because the preferred field itself does not transform as an @xmath
-tensor under a global left @xmath action, which is essential for a
covariant derivative in the setting of differential geometry or a
connection defined in section 1.1.3 .

In complete analogy to the gauging procedure I presented in chapter 2 ,
these “covariant derivatives” can be added to a Lagrangian, which is
invariant under a global @xmath action. If this global symmetry group
@xmath is gauged, the preferred fields disappear from the Lagrangian,
because they are included in the gauge fields of the local @xmath
symmetry. This is the standard Higgs effect.

#### 3.3.4 Problems with a generalization to external symmetries

Neglecting gravitational interactions, Salam and Strathdee investigated
in [ 58 ] if the locally experimentally observed Lorentz isometry @xmath
of our space time could result from a spontaneously broken conformal
symmetry @xmath of space-time. Their result is that all the preferred
fields parametrizing the coset @xmath turn out to be massive in
Minkowski space and some of them have spin. Hence, the interpretation as
“Goldstone bosons” is not as compelling as for the case of internal
symmetries, which is why they also used quotation marks in [ 58 ] . Only
the effect of the linearized @xmath action on preferred fields is the
characteristic one for Goldstone bosons: a constant displacement. ¹⁶ ¹⁶
16 Borisov & Ogievetsky mentioned in a footnote in [ 3 ] that strictly
speaking, the preferred field @xmath ( 3.2.8 ) is the only independent
one with this transformation behaviour. The other one @xmath is a
combination of @xmath and a tensor field.

In a later paper [ 29 ] , they reinterpreted one of these preferred
fields as part of the conformal Minkowski metric @xmath . For such a
curved space-time however, the standard description of a Goldstone boson
has to be treated with care: General coordinate covariance implies by
definition that all coordinate systems are equivalent. If one associates
to every coordinate system a vacuum state, the global symmetry group
@xmath distinguishes these ground states, because @xmath only consists
of the conformal Minkowski isometries, a subgroup of the
diffeomorphisms. Taking e.g. the Einstein-Hilbert action for this
particular metric @xmath as an action, the Lagrangian is invariant under
@xmath , whereas the ground state is not. Hence, the diffeomorphism
symmetry is spontaneously broken by the vacuum state, which merely is an
explicit choice of a coordinate system. As this symmetry however affects
the metric, it is not possible to measure the distance between these
vacuum states in terms of energy as for the case of an internal
symmetry. In other words, the question for the mass of this preferred
field @xmath in this setting is ill-posed.

To conclude, I want to summarize three problems in generalizing the
notion of Goldstone fields to external symmetries:

1.  Salam & Strathdee’s definition of the reducing matrix @xmath assumes
    that it transforms as a tensor under a left @xmath action (eq. (2.3)
    in [ 57 ] ). This is necessary for the preferred field to be an
    @xmath tensor under this transformation. In the present context,
    this is equivalent to fixing the symmetric gauge for the vielbein a
    priori, i.e. already in the affine coset @xmath ( 3.1.6 ). This is
    not natural from a geometric point of view.

2.  Borisov and Ogievetsky argue that the preferred field @xmath (
    3.1.14 ) of the affine coset @xmath corresponds to a Goldstone
    boson, because @xmath must not appear explicitly in an invariant
    action. This refers to the case of internal symmetries again, where
    this is only true for the gauged version, i.e. @xmath , however. It
    is important to note that gauging the affine group produces a
    structure that is different from the diffeomorphism group @xmath :
    although all diffeomorphisms induce a transformation of the vielbein
    @xmath by a matrix multiplication with the Jacobian, which is an in
    general @xmath -dependent @xmath matrix, not all @xmath -dependent
    @xmath matrices in @xmath correspond to diffeomorphisms due to the
    integrability constraint.

3.  Most importantly, the question if the preferred field of the affine
    coset @xmath qualifies as a massless Goldstone boson is ill-posed,
    because different ground states cannot be compared, as there is no
    gauge invariant definition of energy in an arbitrarily curved space
    time.

Hence, although the transformation behaviour of the preferred fields of
the affine coset show the characteristic transformation behaviour of
Goldstone bosons, I do not share the point of view of Borisov &
Ogievetsky that this formal association is compulsory. As far as
experimental physics is concerned, there is no sign of a particle like
the pion associated to these preferred fields @xmath so far.

For internal symmetries, non-linear models with spontaneously-broken
symmetry often are non-renormalizable. This is taken as an indication
that they only describe low energy phenomena [ 30 ] . At higher
temperatures or energies, more symmetry might be restored [ 39 ] . Due
to the problematic definition of energy in the case of external
symmetries, I do not agree with the conclusion in [ 4 , 39 ] either: the
authors argued that the spontaneous breakdown of @xmath to @xmath , with
gravity corresponding only to the @xmath part, should lead to a
modification of gravity by introducing new interaction particles, just
as in particle physics. They conjectured that these “higher spin fields”
corresponded to the other preferred fields of the coset @xmath [ 4 ] .

#### 3.3.5 Technical aspects

I concluded the section 3.1.3 with the reasons why it was necessary to
include the coordinates @xmath in the coset for the case of external
symmetries. Since internal symmetries do not act on the coordinates by
definition, there is no need to include them in a coset of an internal
symmetry group. I emphasize this point, because for external symmetries
both the coordinate indices and the gauge group indices transform in
representations of the same group. This allowed to construct an
antisymmetric Lorentz tensor from the symmetric Lorentz tensor @xmath (
3.1.20a ) that I could then add to the minimal connection @xmath in (
3.2.23 ). It is important to note that this possibility does not exist
for an internal symmetry: there is no way to modify the minimal
connection by the extra part of the Maurer–Cartan form, because the
indices do not transform under the same group.

The definition of a “covariant derivative” from the theory of non-linear
realizations is problematic in the procedure of Borisov & Ogievetsky.
Considering @xmath ( 3.1.14 ) as the preferred field, the definition
reviewed in section 3.3.3 leads to the definition (eq. (26) in [ 3 ] )

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (3.3.8)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

with the abbreviation @xmath as in ( 3.1.20a ). Note that the important
point is hidden in this definition: the @xmath -covariant Lorentz tensor
@xmath is symmetric in @xmath by construction independently of the gauge
of the vielbein @xmath , whereas @xmath is symmetric only for the
special case of a symmetric vielbein gauge, which is necessary for the
interpretation of @xmath as a preferred field. My first reason not to
use this notation of a ‘‘covariant derivative’’ is that it obscures the
fact that the procedure of Borisov & Ogievetsky is independent of the
explicit choice of the vielbein gauge. ¹⁷ ¹⁷ 17 This independence has
already been observed by West [ 64 ] .

The second reason is related to the extraction procedure of the @xmath
-equivariant connection from the class of connections with affine linear
equivariance in section 3.2.5 . To do so, I had to replace the partial
derivative @xmath acting on the unimodular vielbein @xmath by the
minimal connection of the conformal coset @xmath ( 3.2.19 ). This
defines an action of the Lorentz connection @xmath on the symmetric
traceless matrix @xmath by the exponential series ( 3.1.14 ). In
complete analogy, the action of the Lorentz connections @xmath ( 3.1.21
) or @xmath ( 3.2.23 ) on @xmath is defined, too. ¹⁸ ¹⁸ 18 Strictly
speaking, @xmath does not transform as a Lorentz tensor under the affine
group @xmath ( 1.3.18 ), but under its Poincaré subgroup, it does. Any
connection @xmath with @xmath -equivariance canonically induces by
projection a connection with @xmath -equivariance for any subgroup
@xmath . It is however standard in the physics literature, not to
distinguish these two connections by notation. This can be compared with
the definition ( 3.3.8 ) by a direct evaluation of @xmath in terms of
@xmath with the formula ( 3.1.12 )

  -- -------- --
     @xmath   
  -- -------- --

At the first non-trivial order in @xmath , the two objects disagree.
Therefore, the definition ( 3.3.8 ) used in [ 3 ] is ambiguous.

#### 3.3.6 Related work in the literature and @xmath

Borisov & Ogievetsky’s procedure has been generalized by Ivanov and
Niederle [ 31 ] to superspace in the same quantum field theory inspired
way. They applied it to the Ogievetsky-Sokatchev formulation [ 52 ] of
@xmath minimal Einstein supergravity by discussing finite dimensional
superextensions of the affine group @xmath and the conformal one @xmath
.

I will follow a different approach in this thesis. As I have already
discussed in chapter 2 , supergravity can be understood as a special
case of Einstein–Cartan theory, which naturally arises from gauging the
Poincaré isometry group of Minkowski space @xmath . In particular, there
is no need to introduce a super Poincaré group on a superspace.

Where @xmath supergravity is concerned, it is the particular
self-interaction of the three-form potential @xmath ( 2.3.1 ) by a
Chern–Simons term ( 2.3.3 ) that together with the vielbein @xmath
allows for a completion to a supersymmetric theory. Hence, it is very
likely that this coupling is distinguished from others in some group
theoretic way, which might also explain the hidden symmetries reviewed
in section 2.3.4 .

West was the first to connect @xmath supergravity to the procedure of
Borisov & Ogievetsky. In [ 64 ] , he proposed to extend the group @xmath
in the affine coset @xmath ( 3.1.6 ) by a normal subgroup to @xmath in
order to also include the degrees of freedom of the three-form potential
@xmath . In the sight of the chain of hidden symmetries, he further
enlarged @xmath to the group corresponding to the infinite dimensional
Kac–Moody algebra @xmath [ 65 ] . To obtain a consistent global left
action by this group on the coordinates in analogy to the coset
description of section 3.1.2 , he had to generalize the @xmath
representation @xmath of translations to a representation of @xmath .
This formally introduces infinitely many additional coordinates that
might be associated to brane charges [ 66 ] .

West’s algebraic approach will be the guiding principle for the
construction of the exceptional geometry in the next chapter. I will
also combine the degrees of freedom of the vielbein @xmath and of the
three-form potential @xmath into a single coset. To establish the
contact to the dynamics of @xmath supergravity, I will content myself
with discussing the finite dimensional exceptional algrebra @xmath
instead of @xmath . Since the fundamental representation space of @xmath
is @xmath -dimensional, I will also have to introduce additional
coordinates to obtain a consistent left action by the group @xmath on
the coset.

In contradistinction to the algebraic approach used by West, I will
follow a geometric approach, however. In analogy to the definition of
the Poincaré subgroup of @xmath from section 1.3.3 , I will require the
invariance of certain tensors. This will restrict the diffeomorphisms of
a higher dimensional space-time to the appropriate subgroup that
corresponds to the global left action on the coset. In this sense, the
entire coset will have a natural interpretation as a higher dimensional
vielbein in this restricted or exceptional geometry.

## Chapter 4 Maximal supergravity in @xmath

I have concluded chapter 2 with the observation that a @xmath
-dimensional geometry appears to be adequate for the treatment of a
theory that is invariant under an @xmath action on the coordinates and
that contains the dynamics of @xmath supergravity. The @xmath symmetry
of this theory has to be restricted in some way for two reasons.

1.  The degrees of freedom of a sixty-dimensional vielbein without
    further restriction exceed the ones of @xmath supergravity. A
    Kaluza–Klein reduction of an unrestricted @xmath geometry would
    hence imply a coupling to additional physical fields.

2.  A supersymmetry variation in @xmath dimensions would lead to more
    than @xmath supersymmetries in a compactification to @xmath
    dimensions, which is inconsistent as explained in section 2.3 .

This is the reason why I will restrict to an exceptional geometry by
requiring the invariance of a degenerate symplectic form @xmath and a
totally symmetric quartic tensor @xmath of codimension @xmath in the
@xmath -dimensional geometry. This reduces the symmetry group @xmath to
a subgroup, which allows to restrict the degrees of freedom of the
@xmath vielbein and of the supersymmetry parameter in an appropriate
way.

To relate the @xmath vielbein to the bosonic degrees of freedom of
@xmath supergravity, recall from section 2.3.3 that these consist of the
@xmath vielbein @xmath and the three-form potential @xmath ( 2.3.1 ).
West pioneered the idea [ 64 ] to put both fields @xmath and @xmath into
a single coset of @xmath , which I have already discussed in the final
section 3.3.6 of the chapter on non-linear realizations.

An important ingredient in West’s approach is manifest Lorentz
covariance in @xmath dimensions. This is where the present discussion
differs. Following Nicolai and de Wit [ 68 ] , I reduce the covariance
group to @xmath by fixing a block-triangular form for the @xmath
vielbein matrix @xmath , where the @xmath part corresponds to the
codimension @xmath of the tensors @xmath and @xmath .

Since the calculations are quite involved, I have chosen to focus on the
@xmath -dimensional subsector of the exceptional geometry in this
thesis, on which both tensors @xmath and @xmath are non-degenerate. This
will correspond to the @xmath -covariant part of @xmath supergravity. I
will show in section 4.1 that imposing the invariance of @xmath and
@xmath restricts the remaining symmetry group @xmath to @xmath and
@xmath -dimensional translations in accordance with Cartan’s theorem [ 6
] . This fact allows to reduce the degrees of freedom of the @xmath
subvielbein @xmath to the ones of an @xmath matrix.

It also establishes the link to the algebraic approach used in chapter 3
and by West in [ 64 ] . Since the resulting subgroup of @xmath is a
subgroup of the affine group @xmath ( 1.3.18 ), the coset description of
section 3.1.2 can be used without loss of generality. This description
will be the most appropriate one to control the independent degrees of
the vielbein @xmath in a comparison with @xmath supergravity.

Of further importance for the dynamics and for the construction of
supersymmetry variations will be the @xmath -dimensional connection
@xmath , which is the basic building block of any physical theory with a
symmetry. Therefore, I will follow the fate of the result from chapter 3
that the Levi–Civita connection is the unique @xmath -equivariant
connection, whose contorsion @xmath is linear in derivative operators,
if @xmath may only contain vielbein degrees of freedom. An important
insight is that this result does not hold any more, if the equivariance
property of @xmath is restricted to the subgroup of @xmath respecting
the invariance of the tensors @xmath and @xmath . It is this observation
that will allow to define a connection @xmath in @xmath dimensions that
can consistently act on @xmath representations, such as the
transformation parameter @xmath of supersymmetry.

I will proceed as follows. I will start by proving the restriction of
@xmath to a subgroup, if the invariance of the tensors @xmath and @xmath
is imposed. Furthermore, I will also discuss the effect on possible
connections @xmath in section 4.1 . Then, I will parametrize the @xmath
generators by @xmath representations in section 4.2 that is tailored for
a comparison of the @xmath -dimensional vielbein @xmath and of the
connections @xmath with @xmath supergravity in the sections 4.3 and 4.4
. Finally, I will introduce supersymmetry variations on the @xmath
-dimensional exceptional geometry in section 4.5 that exactly reproduce
the ones of @xmath supergravity to the extent that can be expected in
this restriction to the @xmath -dimensional subsector.

I will conclude with remarks on the dynamics and why it looks probable
that all solutions of @xmath supergravity exactly correspond to the ones
of a theory in this sixty dimensional exceptional geometry with @xmath
spacelike Killing vectors.

### 4.1 Exceptional geometry

#### 4.1.1 The symplectic form @xmath

I want to conclude with a group theoretic argument why labelling the

### 4.4 A first comparison to @xmath supergravity

@xmath :

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
                       @xmath   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
                       @xmath   
                       @xmath   
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath

Recapitulating the construction of the exceptional geometry in this

group of the exceptional geometry and there is no reason for the objects
@xmath , @xmath and @xmath ( 4.4.2 ) to transform as tensors under
@xmath , if the dependence on the @xmath coordinates @xmath ( 4.3.1 ) is
neglected.

It is important to observe that this does not falsify the claim that all
solutions of @xmath supergravity are special solutions of a theory in a
@xmath -dimensional exceptional geometry. Solutions are characterized by
their equations of motion and not by the symmetry group of these
equations. The dependence on the additional @xmath coordinates is
similar to the dependence on the mass of a scalar field in flat
Minkowski space that is subject to the massive version of the
Klein–Gordon equation. All fields with the same mass form
representations of the Poincaré isometry group. Only the special fields
with vanishing mass allow for an extension of this symmetry group to
include the scaling symmetry. In the present setting of @xmath truncated
coordinates, the special fields allow for an extension of the @xmath
part of @xmath to @xmath . ²¹ ²¹ 21 In both cases, the equations of
motion impose an additional constraint, of course.

For the proof of @xmath -covariance of @xmath supergravity in [ 68 ] ,
this subtlety is not of importance. De Wit and Nicolai did not claim
that the @xmath -covariance of the equations of motion is related to a
higher dimensional exceptional geometry. They decoupled the right action
on the vielbein from the left one and discussed a general @xmath action
on physical fields @xmath that is not induced by a diffeomorphism. This
fact may have led to the “fixing” of the @xmath gauge of the generalized
inverse vielbein @xmath that they defined in equation (3.5) of [ 68 ] .
It is nice to observe that this object coincides with the corresponding
part @xmath ( 4.3.10 ) of the vielbein in the exceptional geometry.

### 4.5 Supersymmetry

The analysis of section 4.4.2 showed that it is not an appropriate
guideline for fixing the constants @xmath in the general form ( 4.4.10 )
of the connection @xmath ( 4.4.9 ) in the truncation to the seven
coordinates @xmath to require abstract @xmath -equivariance. In this
section, I will show that it is possible to fix @xmath in such a way
that a particular way to act with @xmath on a @xmath representation
@xmath is @xmath -covariant. The result reproduces the supersymmetry
variation of the fermion in @xmath supergravity that corresponds to the
bosonic degrees of freedom @xmath , @xmath , @xmath discussed in section
4.4.1 .

I will conclude with some remarks:

-   The holomorphic supersymmetry parameter @xmath does not introduce
    additional supercharges to the theory. The sixteen real dimensions
    that are spanned by this complex eight-dimensional vector, are
    contained in the @xmath -dimensional real space that is spanned by
    the supercharges of supergravity.

-   Furthermore, I want to address the different representations of
    @xmath that have been used in this thesis: I started with a
    diffeomorphism in the @xmath -dimensional subsector of the
    exceptional geometry. Due to the restricted form of the vielbein and
    the arbitrarily fixed vielbein gauge, this induces an @xmath action
    on the vielbein frame. Hence, the @xmath action on the bosons can be
    presented in terms of real @xmath matrices as used for the explicit
    form of the vielbein in section 4.3.3 . In particular, there is no
    necessity to introduce an imaginary unit @xmath for the bosons. I
    only introduced it in order to make the notation less cumbersome. I
    want to stress that there is no complex structure on the underlying
    manifold.

    The situation is different for the fermions. The endomorphism @xmath
    of the @xmath -dimensional representation space of the Clifford
    algebra in eleven dimensions provides a complex structure. This
    allowed to use the chiral representation of the covering group
    @xmath to define an action on the fermions.

-   The geometric description of the fermions for this subsector of
    exceptional geometry follows the same line of argumentation. A
    fermion @xmath is hence defined as a section of the tensor product
    of a real eight-dimensional vector bundle with the spin bundle in
    four dimensions that provides the complex structure. To obtain a
    full understanding of the geometric interpretation, it is essential
    to discuss the complete theory in the sixty-dimensional exceptional
    geometry.

-   I want to stress that it is not necessary to describe fermions as
    representations of @xmath or @xmath at any stage, because there are
    no diffeomorphisms in the exceptional geometry that would induce an
    action on the vielbein frame that is not in @xmath . Since the
    chiral @xmath action on the fermions @xmath and @xmath includes an
    action on the spin bundle in four dimensions, it is in fact unlikely
    that there is a canonical relation of @xmath and @xmath to @xmath
    representations, because these would not affect the four-dimensional
    spin bundle in general.

## Chapter 5 Conclusion and outlook

West’s idea [ 64 ] to link the formalism of non-linear realization to
supergravity in eleven dimensions proved useful for a construction of an
exceptional geometry. I showed that this formalism provides a natural
tool to describe the independent degrees of freedom of the vielbein in a
restricted geometrical setting.

In this thesis, I focussed on a @xmath -dimensional subsector of the
sixty-dimen- sional exceptional geometry. For the comparison with @xmath
supergravity, it was essential to discuss @xmath representations as
physical fields. In this setting, the class of equivariant connections
@xmath in @xmath was essentially parametrized by two constants @xmath (
4.1.8 ). I used @xmath to define supersymmetry transformations in @xmath
dimensions that link the vielbein to fermions. After a restriction to
the seven common coordinates of the @xmath -dimensional subsector of
exceptional geometry and @xmath supergravity, these supersymmetry
transformations of the fields in this subsector exactly coincide with
the ones of @xmath supergravity, if the constants @xmath are fixed by (
4.5.24 ).

Concerning the @xmath -dimensional dynamics, the fermionic Lagrangian in
this subsector is fixed by @xmath -covariance modulo quartic terms in
@xmath to

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

From a technical point of view, it is highly probable that the same
constants ( 4.5.24 ) for @xmath in @xmath will lead to the fermionic
Lagrangian of @xmath supergravity in a truncation to the common seven
coordinates and fields. For the dynamics of the bosons, the natural
candidate would be the invariant scalar @xmath ( 4.1.27 ) that is
constructed from this connection @xmath by taking the @xmath trace of
its curvature tensor.

The final step to show that all solutions of @xmath supergravity are in
fact special solutions of a sixty-dimensional theory that does not
contain additional fields, would consist in coupling the remaining four
dimensions to the @xmath -dimensional subsector in a consistent way. The
remaining degrees of freedom of supergravity would have to be included
in the sixty-dimensional exceptional vielbein, which may involve further
dualisations.

Another interesting aspect is that the possible appearance of non-linear
terms in fermions is highly restricted. Due to @xmath -covariance, the
following term is the unique choice for a holomorphic contorsion tensor
that is quadratic in @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

Hence, the higher order terms in fermions provide a further consistency
check, if the solutions of @xmath supergravity [ 10 ] are equivalent to
the solutions of a supergravity in a sixty-dimensional exceptional
geometry.

Since the constants @xmath of the @xmath -dimensional subsector of the
theory are uniquely fixed by a comparison to @xmath supergravity, it
would further be interesting to perform a decomposition of @xmath with
respect to its @xmath subalgebra and compare the dynamics and the
supersymmetry variations to IIB supergravity as explained in section
4.2.2 .

My final comment addresses the symmetry structure of an exceptional
geometry. Following the argumentation of section 4.5.4 , I want to
emphasize that the procedure of a joint realization of conformal and
exceptional symmetry does not work in the present context, because a
symmetric tensor is preserved. This fact immediately implies that such a
Borisov & Ogievetsky like procedure can also be excluded to work for an
@xmath construction on a @xmath -dimensional exceptional geometry with
preserved symmetric octic tensor.

It was only after a restriction to the seven common coordinates of the
@xmath -dimensional subsector of exceptional geometry and @xmath
supergravity that the remaining @xmath symmetry was enhanced to @xmath .
¹ ¹ 1 The symmetry transformation that corresponds to the nilpotent
generators @xmath ( 4.2.14 ) is also enhanced in this restriction to the
gauge symmetry of the three-form potential. This procedure was first
observed by West in [ 64 ] . Since it is clear from section 4.2.2 that
the action of the remaining generators of @xmath would violate this
restriction, there is no joint realization of @xmath and @xmath with an
@xmath valued vielbein matrix.

#### 6.1.4 Global properties of the conformal group

I have already mentioned in section 1.2.5 that the same real Lie
algebras do not imply that the corresponding Lie groups coincide. The
same is true for the conformal group. In order to be a subgroup of
@xmath , every element of the orthogonal group @xmath must also uniquely
correspond to a diffeomorphism @xmath that maps the open set (at least
locally) to itself. This is not the case, because for even @xmath , the
two diagonal matrices

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

correspond to the same diffeomorphism @xmath on the @xmath coordinates
by the relation ( 1.3.31 ). ⁵ ⁵ 5 For non-Euclidean signature, i.e.
@xmath , they are even in the same connected component as @xmath . This
implies that for even dimensions, the conformal subgroup of @xmath is
isomorphic to @xmath .

It is obvious from comparing ( 1.3.31 ) and ( 6.1.10 ) that the
inversion ( 1.3.33 ) corresponds to the pair of diagonal matrices @xmath
and @xmath . For even dimension @xmath , both matrices are of
determinant @xmath and therefore, they are not an element of @xmath . As
has a pole, it is not an analytic map. Since @xmath consists of analytic
maps by definition, is not an element of @xmath .

The @xmath argumentation is the same for odd spacetime dimension @xmath
, but in this case the inversion is an element of @xmath . Hence there
is no strict isomorphism between the conformal subgroup of @xmath and
@xmath . This statement is independent of the signature @xmath . There
are elements like in @xmath that cannot be embedded in @xmath . More
details on the conformal group can be found in [ 14 ] .

#### 6.2.1 Clifford algebras

For a diagonal, non-degenerate metric @xmath of signature @xmath , a
Clifford algebra @xmath is a vector space endowed with a multiplication.
It is spanned by the free algebra generated by the objects @xmath with
@xmath modulo the Clifford property

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (6.2.1)
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (6.2.2)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

@xmath are linearly independent of the generators @xmath . It is

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

indices can be adjusted as explained in section 1.4.3 .

@xmath of dimension @xmath from section 1.2.3 can also be transferred to
the Clifford algebra. Hence the generators @xmath are presented as

#### 6.3.1 Proof of theorem 3.2

I want to start the proof with the observation that in a local theory, a
tensor can only be constructed from contracting other tensors and
covariant derivatives thereof. ⁸ ⁸ 8 In particular, integrations are not
admitted in a local theory. Since the minimal connection @xmath ( 3.1.21
) provides a covariant derivative with affine linear equivariance, I can
without loss of generality focus on tensors @xmath that are not of
derivative form.

To discuss the vielbein dependence of @xmath , it is important to recall

  -- -------- -------- -------- --

Next, recall from section 2.2.3 that the fixing of the matrix
representative for the vielbein in the orbit @xmath was arbitrary.
Hence, the tensor @xmath must be independent of the fixing of the
vielbein gauge, i.e. independent of the choice for the matrix form of
the vielbein @xmath . Therefore, the global left action decouples from
the local right action. This implies that Lorentz covariance requires
invariance of @xmath under a global left action by @xmath .

The latter is tantamount to an arbitrary @xmath left action on an
essentially arbitrary @xmath matrix @xmath . Hence, invariance of @xmath

@xmath , the action of @xmath on the variable @xmath is arbitrary. Due
to the locality of the theory however, the only admissible action on the
coordinates is a (multiple) derivative.

Furthermore, recall that global left actions also affect the coordinates
and hence the derivative operators in the coordinate frame @xmath . This

#### 6.3.2 Proof of (3.2.11)

In this section, I will prove that the Lorentz rotation @xmath ( 3.2.3 ,
3.2.7 ), induced by the conformal diffeomorphism @xmath , and the one
@xmath ( 3.2.9 ) compensating a global left action by @xmath coincide.

To start, recall from section ( 3.2.2 ) that after fixing the Lorentz
gauge in such a way that the unimodular vielbein @xmath ( 3.2.4b ) is a
Lorentz representation, the induced Lorentz transformation @xmath was
uniquely determined by the space-time coordinate @xmath and the constant
parameter @xmath alone ( 3.2.7 ). The Lorentz group element @xmath
corresponds via the homomorphism @xmath ( 1.2.3 ) to a Lie algebra
element @xmath , whose generators are antisymmetric with respect to the
Minkowski metric @xmath ( 1.2.9 ). However, there is a unique way modulo
a scalar function how an antisymmetric tensor can be built from two
vectors. And this scalar function is uniquely determined by the
following argument:

The concatenation or group multiplication of two consecutive conformal
diffeomorphisms @xmath implies for the induced Lorentz actions ( 2.2.3 )
with @xmath and @xmath :

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

Since @xmath is an Abelian subgroup ( 1.3.27 ) of @xmath , this equation
takes the form with @xmath and @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (6.3.3)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

As sketched before, the matrix @xmath is antisymmetric and it also is an
analytic function of the two vectors @xmath and @xmath alone. Therefore,
it must have the form

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

with the antisymmetric Lorentz generators @xmath ( 1.3.28a ). Due to
Lorentz covariance, the scalar function @xmath must analytically depend
on the three scalar parameters @xmath , @xmath and @xmath with the
abbreviation @xmath for the contraction with the Minkowski metric @xmath
defined in ( 1.3.35 ). Finally, an iterative evaluation of ( 6.3.3 )
with the Baker-Campbell-Hausdorff formula [ 32 ] uniquely fixes the
dependence of @xmath on these three scalar parameters as a power series.

Hence, the transformation ( 6.3.3 ) uniquely fixes the Lorentz
transformation @xmath that is induced by the conformal diffeomorphism
@xmath and the vielbein @xmath in the symmetric gauge. Thus, it is is
sufficient to show that the same relation ( 6.3.3 ) also holds for the
Lorentz transformations that compensate global left actions on the
conformal coset @xmath ( 3.2.8 ).

A priori, the compensating Lorentz rotation @xmath ( 3.2.9 ) for a
global left action by @xmath on the coset @xmath may depend on all coset
parameters @xmath . The commutation relations ( 1.3.29 ) however show
that in evaluating ( 3.2.9 ), no Lorentz algebra generator @xmath can be
produced by the generators associated to the parameters @xmath and
@xmath . Hence, the compensating Lorentz group element can only depend
on the two vector valued parameters @xmath and @xmath .

Now, it is easy to conclude that the two Lorentz group objects must
coincide: two consecutive global left actions of @xmath with parameters
@xmath and @xmath on the conformal coset ( 3.2.8 ) induce two
compensating Lorentz rotations for the coset. Since @xmath is an Abelian
group ( 1.3.27 , 1.3.25 ), their product must be the compensating
Lorentz rotation of a left action with @xmath . This implies that the
condition ( 6.3.3 ) also is valid for the compensating Lorentz rotations
of the coset. As this fixes the local Lorentz rotation uniquely, the Lie
group elements must be equal

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

if the vielbein matrix is symmetric. If another choice for the vielbein
gauge was fixed, the compensating Lorentz rotation @xmath would also
depend on the unimodular vielbein @xmath explicitly. Since @xmath is not
encoded in the conformal coset @xmath , the equality ( 3.2.11 ) does not
hold for any other gauge choice. ∎

           @xmath   []       @xmath   []

As the second constraint links @xmath to @xmath by the @xmath tensor,
one combination of the two @xmath representation survives, the other one
is discarded. It is obvious that the @xmath tensor cannot link the
@xmath representations. Hence, the second constraint discards both of
them.

To complete the proof, I have to show that there are no polynomials of
higher degree in @xmath that preserve the completely symmetric quartic
tensor. The argument is similar to the one that the group preserving a
symmetric two tensor is finite dimensional. ¹⁵ ¹⁵ 15 As an example
serves the Poincaré group, the isometry group of the Minkowski metric
that I discussed in section 6.1.2 . I start with the quartic tensor in
the coordinate form ( 4.1.5 ).

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
                       @xmath   
                       @xmath   
                       @xmath   
                       @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

Consider its transformation under an infinitesimal diffeomorphism @xmath

  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath      (6.4.3)
  -- -------- -------- -------- -- ---------

with at least quadratic dependence of @xmath on the coordinate @xmath .
Recall that I have restricted the general diffeomorphisms @xmath to
symplectomorphisms @xmath that preserve @xmath ( 4.1.6 ). Hence, the
formula ( 4.1.8 ) implies that the dual coordinate @xmath must
infintesimally transform as

  -- -------- -------- -------- --
     @xmath   @xmath   @xmath   
  -- -------- -------- -------- --

with @xmath at least linear in @xmath . Hence, in the evaluation of the
pull-back, the first term in the quartic tensor will give rise to the
term

  -- -------- --
     @xmath   
  -- -------- --

A brief look at the quartic tensor @xmath in the coordinates @xmath
reveals that no other term in @xmath gives rise to a contribution like
this. Therefore, the quartic tensor cannot be invariant under a
non-constant symplectomorphism that is non-linear in the @xmath
coordinates @xmath .

The same argumentation works for the dual coordinates @xmath . If it was
invariant under such a symplectomorphism that is at least quadratic in
some other coordinates or a combination of coordinates, there must not
exist a linear transformation that maps the coordinates to each other,
because a concatenation would map this again to the case specified in (
6.4.3 ). As the vector space @xmath is an irreducible representation of
the Lie group specified by the linear diffeomorphisms, which is @xmath
what I show in section 4.1.7 , these linear transformations do exist.
This completes the proof that there are no symplectomorphisms @xmath
that preserve @xmath whose Hamiltonian is a polynomial of degree higher
than @xmath .∎

This is a very special case of Cartan’s theorem [ 6 ] that lists the
possible simple, infinite dimensional, non-trivial subgroups of @xmath :
diffeomorphisms of unit determinant, symplectomorphisms and contact
diffeomorphisms.

I conclude by mentioning that this statement does not contradict the
transformation defined in [ 23 ] , because the one in [ 23 ] cannot be
interpreted as a vector field: it is only possible to define a
transformation preserving @xmath that is of at least second order in
derivative operators.
